Tales of the Early Days.
No. XV.—Bill Eastwood’s Scarred Back.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
BY PRICE WARUNG.
The ship Richmond, 512 tons, was, in the
Twenties, on her first voyage to Con victoria. She
had sailed under several flags, and seen much
vicissitude of service. She had carried a “Long
Tom ” during the American war of 1812, and the
interests of a pious firm of Bristol merchants, who
owned a share of her hull, had been so nobly
enriched by her privateering excursions that they
presented a painted window to a church “as a
slight acknowledgment of the goodness of Provi-
dence in blessing them ” at the expense of some of
those “ damned Yankees.” She had, later, also
in the interests of the Bristol firm—peace having
been declared—under the Stars and Stripes,
relieved an Arab slave-dhow of her living cargo,
and disembarked ’em on one of the West Indian
possessions of our Empire which, at that day,
were “ the most lustrous gems in her diadem,”
though to us of this generation they seem but
bloodstains on a map. Then, having served the
cause of an enlightened civilisation in the West,
she, by carrying a battalion of British infantry to
Calcutta, served it in the Golden East. And,
being by this time somewhat leaky and
largely rotten, she was considered worthy of
transporting, not Tommy Atkins to the scenes
where he could be shot down to the glory of God
and the profit of John Company, but convicts.
Tommy Atkins was too important an agent of
Christian cutthroatism for his life to be needlessly
risked, but convicts—well, if the Divine Wisdom
and an ignorant captain ordained that the ship
should never have reached Port Jackson, it is
difficult to say who would have been displeased.
Consequently, the Richmond, being in imminent
danger of condemnation as unseaworthy, was
selected by the Commissioners of Transports to
ta-ke out convicts to N.S.W. There was open
betting by the owner’s clerks as to whether she
would reach Rio.
• * »
She reached Rio, however, and sailed
thence with her water-casks refitted, and with no
more than seven desertions from her crew. Then
the trouble began.
Three days out, the pumps had to be set, and
kept going. The transports, who in any circum-
stances would have been compelled to assist at
the pumps, refused, after a day or two, to furnish
the quantum of workers. Two hundred of them
there were. The guard of soldiers numbered forty,
and the remainder of the crew, seventeen, of
whom three were sick. The officers of the ship,
and of the military, were seven, all told. Then
there were the Surgeon-superintendent, two ladies,
wives of the military officers, and three private
soldiers’ wives. Altogether, a company of two
hundred and seventy souls.
“ Doctor,” said Skipper Scrutton, at breakfast,
one morning, “I requisition a double shift from
the pris’ners for the pumps. My men’ll be
knocked up, what with the ship’s work and the
pumps. One shift a-watch of your fellows isn’t
enough.”
“ Very well,” answered Surgeon Billson. “ But
there is no occasion for alarm, is there?” he con-
tinued, as he observed anxious looks come upon
the faces of the ladies.
“No—none in the least!” the Captain
responded, with a satirical grunt that belied his
words. “ But we can’t be too careful, ma’am,” he
said to the subaltern’s wife, “We can’t afford to
run risks with a craft like this.”
Mrs. Giles strove to laugh off the fear she
evidently felt. “But, Mr. Scrutton, why did you
take command if you didn’t think the vessel safe?”
“ Eh, ma’am ? Why, a man who’s out of a ship,
through no fault of his own, and has younkers to
provide for, can’t pick and choose. He snaps at
the first hulk that offers, if she keeps afloat at
all.”
“ But, why should Government employ such a
vessel?”.
“ Arsk the Gov’ment, ma’am, why it permits
members of the Navy Board—the Board which
charters prison and transport ships—to own odd
sixty-fourths in the crafts they hire. Arsk it
that?”
“Do you mean to say ” broke in Captain
Conyers of the military guard. .
“I don’t mean to say nothin’more,” rejoined
Scrutton, who had received a warning kick from
the Surgeon-superintendent. “ P’r’aps I’ve said
too much. I aint goin’ to quarrel with my livin’,
though it’s only salt boss and hard tack on a
prison ship. An’, doctor, I’ll put those men on at
four bells, if you’ll order ’em up.”
* # #
“Those men,” therefore, were picked out
an hour afterwards. The prisoners were usually
sent on deck (in batches of twenty-five) for an
hour's exercise, but on this occasion there was no
alacrity to pass from the foetid prison to the gang-
ways. The men answered readily enough to their
names when the surgeon called them over at the
muster, but when he proceeded to order forty on
deck and not the customary score and a half, some
new feeling present among the prisoners expressed
itself in a tacit refusal to move.
“Now, then, move along smartly there!’’ex-
claimed the doctor. Up the hatch, my men ! ’
There was no movement, only a nervous rustle as
the men held their breaths.
“What, what’s all this? What, what’s the
trouble?” Billson, easy to get on with, was, like
most “ service trained ” officers, keenly susceptible
to any repudiation or denial of his authority, and
his voice hardened as he grew excited. He was
growing excited—that was evident. He doubled
his “what’s ” when he was losing self-control.
Still, no word.
“ Port messes, 10 to 30, starboard messes, 30 to
50, forward ! ”
The transports shuffled from between the slabs
which formed their mess tables, and stood ranged
in the narrower passageway.
“ Right—face ! ”
A gust of salt breeze came down the hatch, and
many of the fellows, clad only in duck trousers
and woollen shirts, and without socks or shoes,
shivered. Otherwise—no movement.
“What, what? Some more of this damned
stupidity ? O’Brien, what, what’s the meaning of
this?”
O’Brien, “first man ” in “ fifteen ” ones—“ Nos.
10 to 15 hammocks,” saluted, but spoke no word.
“ Damn—this is conspiracy—mutiny!” He
drew out his handkerchief to wipe his face, the pores
exuding the perspiration brewed by hot temper.
•‘Mess, port side, 35, starboard 50, stand out 1”
From the left and the right, the groups of five
moved out —saluted—waited for the command
they designed to disobey.
Billson had not made six voyages in transports
without learning his business. He was not going
to waste time in dealing with the men in lumps.
Bring each person, the individuality of each, to
the heel of authority, and deal with each, and you
get your grip on something gfaspable. You can’t
hold a mob in your hand. It is impersonal and
intangible. So—
“ Port mess 35.” He opened his muster-book
and read the names. “Cox!” The man saluted.
“Hutchinson !” The same respect was elicited.
“ Jones!’’
“ Denny j”
“Brooke!”
Each of the three answered.
“Stand out!”
“ Cox—disclose, instantly, the meaning of this.
What does all this nonsense signify? What,
what?”
The transport paled, but remained firm to the
common determination, whatever it was. He did
no more than repeat his salute.
“ Hutchinson !”
From Hutchinson, from Jones, from Denny,
from Brooke, no response was forthcoming. The
sullenness of the dumb brute apparently held them
all. Two minutes passed.
The Surgeon-Superintendent was gazing in his
wrath at the stubborn throng, and searching in
vain in his recollection fora similar case, when
the Skipper bent over the hatchway :
“Now, doctor, I’m waitin’for those pris’ners.
sent my fellows to breakfast, an’ water’s
coinin’.”
“Captain Scrtßton, send a sergeant guard here,
please !”
“What’s the matter?” Scrutton, prompt to
act, passed the word to Captain Conyers aft, and
then flung himself down the hatch.
“A conspiracy of some sort. They disobey
orders and none o’ them ’ll speak !”
“Eh, boys, that's bad !” exclaimed the skipper.
“ Think what ye’re doin’, lads. What’s wrong?”
Still the obstinate silence —broken only by
the jingle of the guards’ accoutrements and
muskets as they were halted by Conyers at the
edge of the hatch. Conyers glared into the semi-
darkness of the prison-hold, and, in his turn, re-
quested information as to the circumstances.
“Mutiny!” he said, when they had been ex-
explained. “ Mutiny—but this is no time for
mutiny, my lads ! We want help at the pumps,
else we may be water-logged, and have to take to
the boats !” He exaggerated the situation a little,
but he did what the Skipper and the Surgeon
could not do. He obtained a statement as to the
nature of the trouble.
“ That’s just it. But there’ll be no boats for
us ! Go back to Rio.”
It was a loud whisper; perhaps the speaker had
not intended the words to pass beyond his neigh-
bour in the transport ranks; but, whatever his
intention, it reached the ears of the officers, and
of the other transports.
“Who spoke?” demanded the Surgeon. But
he elicited no answer. Silence encompassed them
all again,
“ Order your men down, captain, please,” he
said, after a pause, to Conyers. “We shall want
them, I see!”
And at a word from the officer the soldier*
tramped down,
# # *
Taught by experience, in the insfcdiit that
followed the request for the guards, Billson and
Scrutton, carrying Conyers with them, stepped
backwards out of the prison. Inside, they Would
have been at the mercy of the transports; arid
their presence there would have prevented the
guard from tiring, except in the last extremity.
Outside the prison, even though the prisoners
rushed to the entrance and prevented the closing
of the iron-damped door, the revolters would, by
their numbers and impetuosity, choke the narroW
passage, and thus afford a protection to the officers
till the guards could use musket dr bayonet;
To the surprise of the Surgeon there was, how-
ever, no rush, and the ddor Was clanged td withoilfc
obstruction from the men. As before, the traiis-
ports were dumb and respectful in their demea-
nour, and when the soldiers had ranged themselvies
in three rows of four—no more than four musket
barrels could be forced into the “trap ’ Of tile
prison-door—there was no sound audible save the
wash of the waves against the sides, and half-
suppressed gaspings and bough digs from among
the convicts;
The position of affairs would have been ludi
crous, had it not been for the serious condition
of the ship. As it was, the Skipper and Conyers
were exchanging glances of amusetnbht at the
quandary in which the Surgeon fouhd hiriiself,
when the mate came to the hatch way and reported
to Scrutton that the water Was Ihcreasing alarm-
ingly in the well. The Skipper then asked the
Surgeon to make one more appeal for hands fdr
the pumps.
‘‘Tell 'em the whole truth, doctor! Thete’s
evidently some controlling mind there who is not
eager for extreme measures and will see the
reasonableness of the thing.” And the doctor
agreed. The heat of his irritation had subsided,
and he was arguing with himself as to whether,
after all, he would be justified in ordering a volley
to be fired when the mutiny was as yet only con-
structive and not actual. So he flung open the
trap and peered into the gloom of the prison.
“Men,” he said, “whoever spoke just now is
not altogether right. I see what the trouble is—
you’ve heard something of the condition of the
vessel. You think we can’t reach Sydney, and
you wish us to go back to Rio. You’re afraid if
we had trouble in mid-sea you would have no
chance of getting at the boats ”
He paused, as though to secure a confirmation or
denial of his remark ; but neither came. The men
held themselves in the bonds of their agreement,
whatever it was.
“Now, men,” he went on with something more
of temper in his speech, “ I don’t wish to treat
you as mutineers, or as children, but as reasonable
beings. We can’t go back to Rio—my duty and
that of Captain Scrutton is to take you direct to
Sydney. And you’ve seen enough of me by
this time to know I’m a man of my
word. What I say I’ll do. And I say
now, if we have to take to the boats, I’ll be the
last man to leave the ship. If there are not boats
enough for us all, and some have to stay behind,
I’ll be one. Isn’t that fair ? ”
His tones were honest, and carried conviction,
except to those second voyage fellows who by
some hideous experience or other had been ren-
dered impenetrable to belief in the straight-
any official of the System. And,

I
though there was no articulate response to his
words, a sudden murmur that might have become
articulate had it not been instantly checked, arose
from the rear of the prison.
“What, what, isn’t that fair, I say ? There are
some second-trip men among you, and though
none have travelled with me before, I ask them
have they ever found a fairer man? You ask
them, you others, have they ? ”
Still, the curious, absurd but dangerous silence.
“Have I ever played into the hands of the
owners’ Did I ever profit by ‘eights ’?;• Did 1
keep my men on short rations, or short clothes,,
and sell surplus stock ? Did I ever stint veget-
ables and lime-juice ? Haven’t I had many a row
with the skippers over letting so many prisoners
go without irons early in the voyage ? You know
it is as I say, that I’ve always been fair ”
“ He hanged a pris’ner at the yard-arm once.”
A whisper, like the first.
“Yes, I did! What, what if I did? And
another time I shot a man just a prison-trip like
this. I punished them to save other fools from
treading in their footsteps. And I’d punish your
leaders, too, if I knew who they were ”
He was interrupted by jeering laughter, though
the transports still held back their speech.
« * *
Nevertheless, as there is a laughter which
is more eloquent than speech, they had spoken
when they laughed into the practised ear of
Surgeon Billson. The laughter told him that they
had resolved among themselves to maintain
silence, so that no evidence of leadership should be
produceable against one or two or three of their
number.
The blood-penned chronology of the System
included countless dates when the spokesmen of
convict parties had been treated as ringleaders.
The jeering chorus was so many words to say,
“Not one of us, nor two, nor three shall be held
responsible for this outbreak of constructive
mutiny, but, if any, all.”
“That’s it, is it?” cried Billson, “I see now!
You’ve got some old lag, some second or third
tripper ainong you who is a gallows-lawyer, and
he has put you up to keeping your mouths shut,
so I shan’t know your leaders. But that won’t
go down with me, lads—not for a second.
Now,” —he took out his watch—“ Captain Conyers,
what time do you make it?”
Conyers made it 11.5 ; the doctor’s lever made
it 11.7. “I’ll go by your watch, Captain,” Billson
resumed. “They’ll have two minutes longer,tp
think of it.” He paused.
There was a rustle of expectation in the prison,
and a talking as though the men were drawing near,
the trap to hear his ultimatum. The doctor,
noticed the movement; and to emphasise the
effect of his next words, drawled them with slow,
precision.
“Take—notice —that—at —the —hour —of—noon
—by Captain Conyers’ watch —I shall —un-
less—”
He held them in suspense. He was experiment-
ing upon their nerves, and trusted that some of
the weaker would give way under the stress and
break forth into reviling. That was what hp
wanted. He did not care who it was so long as
he could formally charge somebody with the
serious offence of turbulent and rebellious speech.
Having the somebody, he would tjien, seize him
as ringleader and inciter to the mutiny, and
the issue would be simple whether he had been
fortunate enough to capture the right man or
not. If the right one, punishment would be in-
flicted upon the deserving; if the wrong one, he
knew sufficient of the convict nature to understand
that the mass would be unwilling to let the inno-
cent suffer. And so he held the prisoners on
tenter hooks. No sudden cry, however, fell from
the convict throng, though the stress and strain
was intense. What did he mean to do?
“ Unless . Ah, Mrs. Giles, come to see
what we are about?” He broke off to exchange a
few remarks with the ladies, who; made anxious
by the delay in tffe re-appearance of Captain
Conyers, had walked forward. Minutes passed
before he resumed. Still the men rejected the
temptation to exclaim. Whoever their leaders
were, the transports had been trained to unusual
self-control.
“Unless—at twelve o’clock 1 say—you men do
one of two things : tell off a working-party, for
the pumps, or give up your leaders—l shall fire—
a volley—into your midst. Captain Conyers—the
time again, please.”
“ Thirteen minutes past eleven !”
The Surgeon and Scrutton went on deck.
* * #
Conyers stayed at the mouth of the prison
with his guard. In a few minutes, however, he
was relieved, his subaltern, Giles, coming down
with a message from the Surgeon-Superintendent,,
requiring his presence on, deck. But in a little
while, and apparently as a result of; the confer-
ence, he re-appeared at the hatch, and ordered the
guard on deck.. As the soldiers mounted and.
lined up, the Captain, with a- slow distinctness of
voice, which reached the prisoners grouped nearest
the trap in.the prison-door, ordered them-to draw
their charges. The prisoners, intent upon, de-
velopments, at this- lost for a moment their
self-control. They were at no loss to under-
stand why the charges should be withdrawn-
just when it was expected- that a deadly
volley would be requisitioned. It did not
signify a change in. the Surgeon’s policy—it
simply meant a. new precaution. The soldiers
had loaded, their muskets, perhaps two,, perhaps
three days,ago ; and when it came to the pulling
of the trigger, there might, be no certainty of. dis-
charge. A, new charge meant,—business.
This the transports knew. The second and
third trippers,amongthem advised the “raw’uns”
on the point. Apd : the “ raw ’uns ” grew cowed,
and would have given ip, but for the graduates in
the lore of the lag. These —half-a-dozen or, so-
swayed the rest of the two hundred. They trusted
tp their lpck, It, was. quite unlikely, wasn’t it,
that having survived the System so far, they
would suQcumb to the bullets of a ship’s company]?
No ; the bullets would find billets assuredly in the
bodies of the first And after the volley
was fired, the fear,of comment and publicity which,
since there had-beena free press in Sydney, invari-
ably weighed upon the official mind afloat, would
produce a reaction, and the skipper, and the
surgeon would take the vessel back to Rio after,
all, And then, hey, ho ! fpr escape!* This was
the old lags’game planned from the first, as, soon
as they had heard frpm the cony.ict steward, who
had heard.the conversation, at, the cabin-table* as-
to the ship’s, condition. “ Wje’ll give ’em a
workin’-party to Rio an’ now’ere else !” said one
of the oldsters to a raw ’un, who ventured to
suggest a compliance with the surgeon’s demands.
“ An’we’ll keep our jaws tight till they consent
to take us back.”
“Why not tell ’em so, then,” said the “raw
un,” “ before they fire?”
“Who’s to tell them? If we all shout it—
they’ll say they ’erd some one voice distineter
than the rest, an’ they’ll ’av that chap out as
ringleader. An’ they can ’ang a cove for mutiny
in Rio as well as in tfa’ ol’ town. No —they can’t
’ang two ’undred of us any’veres. But they can
’ang one or two ’ere it pleases ’em.”
And so the oldsters had their way. Experience
has a more servile following among the evil than
among the good. Which, being a patent and
read-as-you-run truth, has never yet been appre-
hended by the intellect of the administrators of
British penal systems.
» # #
At eight bells the guards were re-marched
to the hatch, and Conyers addressed them in a few
words that were plainly audible to the prisoners.
“ Men,” he said, “you may have an unpleasant
duty before yon, but you will do it, I doubt not,
as you have performed duties where there, to your-
selves, were greater risks. I would, for your sake
and mine, that we ran no- risks now —I don’t like
to shoot men down in cold blood,, but that is our
duty now. We must have labour for the pumps;
and: if we had not that pressing need we cannot
allow these prisoners to defy discipline.” Ho
paused before he concluded. And the heads of a*
dozen, men, pressed close against the bars of the
trap.in the prison- dbor, shivered with-an involun-
tary creeping of the skin as his last words rang,
out—
“ With ball-cartridge—load ! ”
©f course the men inside the trap could not see
the broad smiles that spread over the faces of the
guard,
* *
The guard:, led by Conyers and the Surgeon,
descended. Dr. Billson, after a second’s delay,
spoke to the transports.
“Will you give up your leaders—or send out a-
working-party and obey orders ? Which ? ”
Again—only the silence for his-answer !
“ For the second time I- ask you—which ? The
third time, if you refuse, I shall fire ! ”
And, still he was mocked by. the strenuous dumb-
ness.
“For the third and last time—which will you-
do?”
“No answer? Then—Captain Conyers; pur-
suant to my instructions; I ask you to do your
duty ! ”
The dbctor dtew back a-step, and Conyers took
his place by. the trap.
“ Prisoners—think,!' Will you obey the doctor’s-
orders ? ”
He waited, while you could' have counted 1 ten.-
Then—
“ Guard, by sections of four—advance-fire
retire —and re-load.” The first section advanced'
and, dropped their muskets.
“ Men —once more ”
“No?” Conyers went on.- “Then, guards,-
through the bars. Ready ! ”
He drew-back; the soldiers - rested'the rims of
their barrels on the ledge of the trap— a barrel to ■
every aperture in the barred trap.- There was no
need to aim.
“Ready! Fire!”
* *' *•
But before the second' syllable of the 1
“ Ready ’’ had merged into the terrible word, andf
while the prisoners had rushed to the sides of theif
hold, as though they could escape from the im-
pending volley, one voice, at least, was heard
from atr.ong them.
“You —— butchers ! If you will shoot, shoot
me ! ” And the broad back of one of the greatest-
scoundrels of the lot was thrust against the trap—
and the rims. But too late to avoid the “Fire !”
# # «
The gory spectacle that Bill Eastwood
presented to the horrified “raw” prisoners, was'
an object-lesson in the System’s discipline. And
Bill Eastwood, on his third voyage out, being
unable longer to preserve with menace or coax-
ing,, his moral sway over the mass, the pumps got
their working-party, andjthe ship, after a seven-
months’ voyage, duly reached Sydney.
Had the guard really loaded with ball, there
would, of course, have been no necessity to' amend*
Bill’s “ marks/’ As it Was, having loaded only
with blank cartridge, the wadding and flame of
the muskets left him, not with life only, but'with 1
certain sears that appeared iii: the records for'
many long years after - or didn’t appear, accord-
ing, as to whether the record-clerk was lazy or
industrious. Bits of wadding and fibres of woof
came out of Bill at intervals, for some months.-
When he left the Richmond' for the hospital, atr
Sydney, Surgeon-Superintendent Billson read to 1
him his amended description :
1 ‘ Height, S' feet inches ;■ complexion, tudd'y ;-
hair, sandy brown; mixed with grey eyes, blue ;-
general- remarks, sandy whiskers*; eyebrows light,'
hands hairy, tattooed anchor and meifibaid, slight
scar on back of forefinger of left hartd,- scar oh
ball of left thumb. That’s the Old lot, Eastwbod 1
Now for the new ” -
“Aye, -aye, let’s-ha’ ’em too. I’ll know myself ! ,v
growled Bill.
“Back, greatly scarred from wadding, fired at
point-blank.”
“ By a butcher of a- sawbones,” said Bill.
Dr. Billson laughed', as he could afford to do;;
having quelled a-dangerous and singular mutiny
at no more expense than the four blank cartridges-
and Bill Eastwood’s scarred back.

Thousands of acres of splendid arable land!
between Bathurst and Orange (N.S.W.) are over-
run with the sweet-briar pest, which gives the
country from the distance the aspect of uncleared 1
scrub-land. A correspondent urges that the-
landowners should be compelled to* eradicate the
weed, or be heavily taxed.

that he is practising as a Specialist, to be appreciated as a benefactor to suffering mankind. As the
greater portion of the New South Wales public is aware, Dr. Kelly is a Legally Qualified and Registered
Physician, and has by far the largest practice in the colonies. Many persons at a distance, however, are
unaware of his great success, as, hitherto, he has refrained from advertising. Hence, to widen the
sphere of his usefulness, he has determined, by means of advertising, to make himself known in remote
districts, where his reputation may not yet have travelled. As the question may arise, why does so
eminent a Physician as Dr. Kelly find it necessary to advertise, he may reply to one and all as
follows:—That the Physician who has the power to dure, and remains silent, is guilty of an un-
pardonable sin of omission; for if there is a person on earth to be pitied, it is he "'ho is obliged to
associate with his fellow beings while his mind is constantly harassed and tortured

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 2(5, 1892.
The Apostate in Ermine.
Sir Samuel Griffith has made his farewell
to las Australian political life, and retires to
the safe asylum of a Chief Justiceship, with
£3500 a year, and travelling expenses, and
a prospective pension. There are people
who will envy him. The Bulletin would
as soon think of envying the leper who last
week carried his living corpse to the lazaret.
The leper has consolations that are denied
to the political apostate of Sir Samuel
Griffith’s stamp. The merciful tenderness
of Nature numbs the faculties as the blood
grows foul, and the plague-smitten wretch
bears stolidly his hideous burden along the
road that leads to the final oblivion, without
ever knowing the full horror of the thing that
has come upon him. But there is no such
anodyne for the other. He must rob, and
shudder as he rots. So we pity him.
# # *
There was a day when the name of Samuel
Walker Griffith stood for big possibilities.
How far he would go no one could tell.
Without any burning enthusiasm for human-
ity, he had still the clear, cold intelligence
that sees far ; he had the scientist’s pulseless
passion for mathematical regularity and
appointed order. Not with boiling iudigna-
tion at the wrongs of the toiler groaning
under the yoke of Christian civilisation, but
with the deliberate precision of a botanist
classifying and arranging the specimens that
some child has jumbled, he set himself to
disentangle the knotted threads of our social
order. Ho might, one day, have been the
brain of a great movement that would stir
humanity to its base—the thinking-machine,
controlling and regulating and directing the
forces of whole armies of strong limbs and
beating hearts. In this sense he might have
looked to be a leader of n.en. That was his
dream. Pie looked into the future, aud saw
a federated Australia grappling in the might
of its lusty youth with the monsters before
which the old, grey nations cower appalled,
reading the riddles of the Sphinx, andshouting
the good tidings of a new hope to the weary,
toilworn masses in the lauds across the sea ;
and high above it all, in the seat of honour
and of pride, he saw—it doesn’t matter now
what he saw. Samuel Walker Griffith,
the apostate, has tumbled down to the bench
<>f a provincial law-court. And we pity him.
* # *
This man's unhappy career is a warning to
those who know not how to wait. Griffith
saw the evils of laud-monopoly and chartered
privilege, and years ago he fought strenu-
ously to defeat, and did defeat, the land-
grab railway swindle promoted by M‘ll-
wraith and Co. It was a voice crying in
the wilderness against the alienation of
Crown lands before the Single-tax league
had cut its first teeth. With a clear fore-
sight of all that the Kanaka slave-trade
meant —that it was putting in the hands of
the nigger-driver of to-day a new lash for
the back of the white slave of to-morrow —
he led a victorious crusade with the watch-
word, “Australia for the white man 1” It
was decreed that the Kanaka slave-trade must
stop. Then he turned his eyes to the white
slave-trade. “Freedom of contract!” he
cried scornfully. “There is no such thing
between employer and employed. The
weaker party is driven to accept the terms
which the stronger party chooses to give.
Why should the labourer not share in the
profits that the capitalist now seizes ? Why,
rather, should he not have them all ? ” That
was the Samuel Walker Griffith of 1888.
And he looked to see the labouring masses
honour him as a god. But they turned away
and worshipped at other shrines. If he had
waited, some day he would have got his
reward for the things he did. But he could
not wait; his heart turned to gall and bitter-
ness, and he wheeled round savagely and
undid it all. Freedom of contract! he drove
it in at the point of the bayonet. The
Kanaka slave-trade ! he opened wide the
flood-gates and let the black tide in.
Land - alienation ! he handed Queens-
land over to the tender mercies of
the dummyist. Land-grab railways ! he
forced them on a protesting country with a
brigade of dumb, servile votes in Parliament
and the inexorable gag. Then, with all the
work of his life in ruins, he turned his back
on the smouldering wreck, and slunk away
with his thirty pieces of silver in his rene-
gade apostate hand. His Honor Sir Samuel
Griffith has £3600 a year ; but his Honor
Sir Samuel Griffith has also a ghost that
will never leave him asleep or awake —the
phantom of what he was, of what he might
have been. So let us pity him,
* # #
Life has many things in store for the man
of Griffith’s talents. They are qualities
that bring a big price in the market where
their owner goes a-huckstering. Sir Samuel
may yet change his knighthood for a peerage.
Hands like his will find it not impossible to
shape the judicial bench into a ladder to the
Privy Council and the House of Lords. He
need not want for gauds to. blazon his dis-
honour. But the Dead Sea fruit is dust and
ashes, gild it how you will. Even if he carry
the dregs of his spoilt life to the woolsack,
he can never hope again to share even the
leper’s untroubled content, unless, indeed,
the future holds for him the leper’s boon of
dulled sensibility and poppied forgetfulness.
We have no tribute for him now but pity—a
pity that the tragic squalor of his fallen for-
tunes makes too mournful for contempt.
The Biggest Land-Grab on Earth.
As we go to press the Queensland Legis-
lature, on the invitation of the Criffil-
wraith Cabinet, has given its cordial assent to,
the construction of no less than eleven land-
grant railways in one stupendous job. This
will probably be the last act of the worn-
out Parliament. The difficulties in the way of
this mammoth grab have been very great,
but, so far as appearances go, they have all
been surmounted. The country has twice de-
clared emphatically against the land-grant
system, and both MTlwraith and Griffith
got into power chiefly because they pledged
their non-existent honour and their bloated
reputations never to touch or handle or encourage
or be in any way accessory to the unclean finan-
cial thing. The great majority—probably 75
per cent. —of the present Assembly took the
same pledge. The Parliament, by its own vote,
was dead two years ago ; it has passed an Act
whereby the duration of Parliaments is limited
to three years, yet it has now been almost five
years in existence ; therefore, the very fact that
it exists at all is an act of gross and shameless
treachery. The only way to complete the fraud
is to crowd the eleven railway agreements
through in red-hot haste in the fag-end of
the last session, before the House becomes
extinct in law as well as in equity, and a new
Assembly arises to repudiate the land-grant
brigands and all their works. Also, the two
chief traitors —the one with the haggard, con-
sumptive eye, and the adipose man with the
stertorous breath are, to all appearance)
slowly dying like the Legislature which
supports them. But none of these difficulties
have been sufficient to block the way. The
Coalition leaders have shamelessly apostatised.
The Parliament has apostatised likewise almost
as one man, and there is good reason to suspect
that most of the renegades are in the land-grab,
in one shape or another —some of them in many
shapes. The majority has decided to cheerfully
ignore the fact that their House is practically
dead by its own act. And even if the great
job is only shunted through five minutes
before the Legislature dies, there seems very
little reason to doubt that it will be shunted
through all the same,
* m m
The business of the session is practically
this ; (1) To bind the province to the con-
struction of II railways, many of which cannot
be built for over 20 years to come. (2) To make
a cast-iron agreement whereby the syndicates
which construct these railroads shall receive the
full value of their lines on an inflated
estimate made practically by themselves —in
land, which land may be rated as low as 2s. 6d.
per acre, being, perhaps, one-eighth its value ;
and at the same time shall have the sole use of
the lines for ten years, at the end of which
period the shoddy and partially worn out pro-
perty shall be sold to the State at its full, or
more than its full, price as a new article. In
other words, the company which builds a rail-
way worth £1,000,000 may get land to the
nominal value of £1,000,000, but probably,
including minerals, to the real value of
£8,000,000 or more—and will also own the rail-
way, with power to sell it when the rails are
getting worn out and the plant rusty, to the
State for another £1,000,000 or upwards,
whereby the country will get its own
£1,000,000 worth of line for something like
£9,000,000. There are one or two alternative
systems, one of which is, so far as can be ascer-
tained from the confused and badly-drafted
bill, that the country may take over its railway
without any cash payment, provided it gives
the company twice its nominal value in land—
which may mean about sixteen times its real
value—and allows the company to monopolise
the line for fifty years; but they all amount to
the same steal in the end. (3) To ratify another
cast-iron agreement whereby the State pays for
all surveys, thus relieving the syndicates of a
preliminary expense of about £250,000. This
is an extensive programme for a short session—
fifty years of hard, unblushing fraud and con-
fiscation crowded into something like four
weeks, but the prospects of defeating the
measure are apparently nil. The only thing
which remains to be done is the final ratifica-
tion of the agreements with the syndicates, and
if the agreements can only be got ready in time
a special session of Parham nt will put them
through in short order.
# # #
The syndicators who will profitby this swindle
are mostly South Queensland financiers ; the one
or two hundred million acres of rich mineral and
agricultural land —probably among the richest
in Australia—which the syndicates are autho-
rised to grab are mostly in the coming province
of the North. Separation is now admitted to
be inevitable, therefore the Brisbane specula-
tors propose to bleed the North to the very
last drop of blood and the last speck of gold
before that event takes place. When Separa-
tion comes, and North Queensland starts busi-
ness for itself as an independent province, it
will be utterly and irretrievably ruined. Its
lands —for the Bill actually permits the possible
seizureof nearly one-half the province, its mineral
deposits, its railways, its rivers will be owned
by the Criffilwraith railway kings. It will
have no land revenue, no mineral resources save
the scrapings of its old goldfields, no room for
expansion—nothing save the hideous liability
to be incurred in the re-purchase of the laird-
grant railways, sundry malarious, fever-ridden,
snake-haunted wastes about the Gulf, and
those deserts which the syndicates didn’t
take because they were not worth taking. It
will be swamped by cheap paupers, imported
by the syndicates to occupy and give additional
value to their stolen lands. It will be bossed
by the syndicates ; the votes of some 2000
miles of railroad and 100,000,000 or 200,000,000
acres of land, and the personal suffrages
of the syndicators and their dependants,
hangers-on, employes, and pauper settlers will
man down the scanty population, and command
a majority vote in the Legislature. The
country will be ruined and over-run by Chinese,
Kanaka, and similar immigrants brought in by
the syndicates to keep wages down, because as
wages go down land values go up. The syndi-
cates will command the means of locomotion,
and—as the experience of the United States has
demonstrated —the command of the means of
locomotion is the ownership of the earth. And,
in short, the Criffilwraith endowed railway
syndicates will own the earth and the waters,
and have a lien upon the atmosphere, and the
bodies and souls of the nation will lie theirs,
» * #
The only remedy for the tremendous evils that
must come upon the North when the Criffil-
wraith land-grant device gets into working order
lies with the North itself. When Separation is
secured, the first act of the new Legislature
should be to tear up the Land-grant Railway
Bill, to repudiate the syndicates and all their
works, to. revoke the grants, stop the surveys,
aud wipe out the whole robbery in one act. If
it is enacted in the North Queensland Consti-
tution—as it certainly will be—that the North
takes over all the responsibilities put upon it by
the old Brisbane Legislature, then the Consti-
tution must be promptly amended, or if it can-
not be amended it must he torn up also. I he
case is so utterly fraudulent that no oaths or
parchments or Acts of Parliament should be
allowed to stand in the way. The sanctity of
pledges deliberately entered into by the people’s
alleged representatives, and ratified by the
Cabinet and the Sovereign’s deputy, is not suffi-
cient to cover such an enormity. Even the
Englishman, with his patient, ass-like reverence
for every State document which has been
engrossed on sheepskin, and signed by a duly
constituted authority, and decorated with a
huge plug of red sealing-wax, would admit as
much in similar circumstances. If any British
Legislature and Sovereign, being led away by
the hunger after hard cash and possessed of the
devil of unscrupulous speculation, were to enact
in legal form, that all Great Britain and Ireland
were to be given away gratis to a Chinese or
other foreign syndicate, all the reverence in his
patient soul would not prevent him repudiat-
ing the contract without compensation, and
dethroning the monarch who sanctioned the
ghastly monstrosity, and gaoling, for high
treason, the Ministry that propounded it, and
every legislator who lent them his support
or countenance. The present case is
exactly parallel except in one essential:
the Griffith-MTlwraith Coalition deliber-
ately proposes to give away, absolutely for
nothing—for the railway which is supposed to
be the equivalent has to be paid for afterwards
quite apart from the land-grant—an area vary-
ing, under the alternative agreements, from
one-and-a-half to three times the size of the
British Isles. Therefore, the fraud upon the
present and the coming generations is so much
the greater.
# # #
And not only would North Queensland, and,
indeed, all Queensland, be justified in tearing
up the contract with the Criffilwraith syndi-
cates, but it would also be justified in denoun-
cing those w r ho had consummated the job as
public enemies, in impeaching them for treason
to the Commonwealth, and in dragging down
Samuel Griffith from his seat on the judicial
Bench as one who had demonstrated his
unworthiness for the office he assumed. And
if the Imperial courts should be appealed to
and should give judgment against the State,
and the Imperial power should he enlisted—an
extremely improbable contingency—in support
of that judgment, then there remains one last
alternative—armed resistance. However hope-
less such a course might be, it would be easy to
justify it so far as the unwritten code of
patriotism goes. History is full of reverence
for patriots who fought to save a kingdom, and
there is the raw material for three kingdoms in
this colossal land-steal.
They Meant Well, but They Didn’t
Know.
Editor Bulletin, The New South Wales
Legislative Assembly has just turned out
another Electoral Bill. It ought to be a
good one. If any legislature in the world
might be supposed to have attained com-
petence to construct an Electoral Bill it is
this one. For something like five years it has
been practising the art and mystery of electoral
law making. Its members have talked reams
and volumes in debating one electoral bill after
another. The outcome of this long and severe
experience is now on its way to the Legislative
Council, very possibly to be received with
derision and dismissed with ignominy. Of
course, the Council may “ amend ” the bill,
There are instances in which the Council
amends a bill passed by the Assembly very
much in the fashion employed by Bluebeard to
correct a wife. In other instances it treats the
Assembly’s offspring in the spirit of the wicked
uncle of romance. It receives it with every
appearance of respect, but hands back a
hideous changeling which the parents refuse
to recognise. Under present circumstances,
there may be reason for believing that
the Assembly’s little bill will be accorded
at least the concession of a decent reception,
A measure which embodies the fruits of labour
so long, consideration so deep and so protracted,
and wisdom so cultivated, can scarcely be
thrown contemptuously on to the dust-heap.
During the course of the reiterated discussions
which have taken place in the New South
Wales Assembly, there have been reviewed
almost every principle which could by any
ingenuity be conceived to attach to the question
of representation. On the whole, those dis-
cussions have resulted in the adoption of certain
theorems, by common consent. Not indeed
quite unanimously, because there have been a
number of members holding off in a sort of
sulky aloofness, who are understood to believe
that the only fault of the existing electoral law
is that it gives the common people (who have
no stake in the country) too large a share of
influence.
# * *
But, apart from such gentry, whom nothing
would satisfy save a despotism with themselves
for despots, there has been a pretty general
agreement that every man in tire community
should have one vote for a member to represent
him in the Legislature, and that no man should
have more than ono. Moreover, that every
vote should have precisely equal value and
effect. Also, that at a general election all the
elections should take place on one day. Fur-
ther, that no man should be precluded from
offering himself as- a candidate for election
merely on account of having no ready money.
It is generally agreed that by the realization of
these three essentials the progress of Democracy
will be ensured and accelerated. I say, it
is generally agreed : that doesn’t involve your
own concurrence. Let us examine.
# * #
Take first the celebrated “
vote.” This has become a sort of sacred phrase,
instinct with holy virtues, which it would be
sacrilege to question. The way in which some
people have come to say this phrase, as if it
were a short prayer or invocation, useful for
exorcising evil political spirits, makes one want
to call for the steward and a basin. It re-
calls the old Scotch woman and the blessed
word “Mesopotamia.” You have always been
in favour of the abolition of plural voting, such
as has been permitted by the present electoral
law of New South Wales. It was one of your
erstwhile colleagues and comrades in The
Bulletin who first proposed it to the New
South Wales Legislature. But with the sense-
less idea that every man in the community has
an equal right and fitness to exercise the
franchise—which seems to be the form the
theory takes in sundry ill-instructed heads
—no intelligent man can have any pati-
ence. The very contrary I hold to be
the case. The fitness of men to exercise
the electoral franchise is just as varied as their
fitness for writing an essay, or leaching a
class, or cleaning a watch, or driving a


steam-engine, or composing an air. The ideal
electoral law would be one which gave every
man a vote and some men from two up to a
thousand, according to their capacity for
making good use of the privilege. The fellow
who gives his vote to Jones because that candi
date sings a good song, and shouts glasses
round at the local pub. should be overborne by
the extra vote of the fool who gives his
suffrages to Smith because Smith makes long
and earnest speeches which seem wise because
he—the man with two votes—can’t understand
them, and because he consequently believes that
Smith will prove a valuable legislator. And
that well-meaning fool’s two votes ought to he
obliterated by the three votes of the man who
knows that Jones is a shallow windbag and is
unable to recognise the superior intelligence of
Smith. And the whole series of these and
similar voters with their single, duplicate
and multiple votes should be overborne
by the thousand votes of, say, the
late Archbishop Whately or Cardinal New-
man or John Stuart Mill. I had
nearly written “or Francis' Bacon.” But
the occurrence of his name serves perfectly
to illustrate the reason why this plan of plural
votes cannot be carried into practice. Bacon
had an intellect perfectly monumental. The
combined sense of a whole generation could not
weigh against it. But he was a rogue. It
would mar the whole system to confer multi-
plicity of votes on the wisest man on earth if he
were not as virtuous as he was wise. That’s
where the difficulty comes in. The honest man
is, as often as not, a very dull fellow. The man
of talent is, as often as not, a selfish, rascally
scamp. Plural voting is simply a widely-
diffused or diluted form of despotism. No
better form of human government can be
conceived than a benevolent despotism. But
experience, running back to the very beginnings
of history, has proved that while it is tolerably
easy to get a despot, a benevolent despot is to
be sought only in heaven.
# # #
The fact is, that no human judgment could
suffice to distinguish what men were entitled,
by their possession of wisdom and virtue com-
bined, to exert more power than their fellows.
And, failing such distinction, it is better to
submit to the evils which may result from an
equal allotment of political power, than to
aggravate them by giving extra influence to
men who may be rather less than more qualified
to make a good use of it. The idea that
possession of property indicates political virtue,
which lingers in so many electoral systems, is
intrinsically false and dangerous. Acquisitive-
ness is not ranked among the virtues in any
moral code. It. is generally denounced as a
vice, and in no code is it more severely repre-
hended than in that of Christianity. Besides,
property never fails to exert an influence
inherent in its very nature. Therefore, that
influence —seldom exerted for the general wel-
fare, but commonly for the accomplishment of
selfish ends—requires rather special counter-
action than special augmentation.
* # #
The theorem that every vote should have
exactly equal value has found expression in the
New South Wales Assembly’s bill, in the pro-
vision for equal electorates. Two thousand two
hundred electors anywhere are to elect one
representative. The result will not neces-
sarily be that there will be equality of voting
power exercised. Mill has examined this
position and has challenged its accuracy.
Gladstone has likewise impeached it. But,
after all, it does not greatly matter. Absolute
equality of influence eludes the voter in any
case. Unless all elected representatives were
of equal capacity and talent, no such equality
can be secured. The influence of the two
thousand,! wo hundred voters who will constitute
an electorate may be reduced to nullity by
their choice of a feeble representative, or it
may be hugely magnified by their selection of
a man of exceptional parts. The electorate
which has a minister for member exerts far
more influence than the electorate that has a
muff. Thus, dispassionately examined, it
therefore appears that single electorates will
not secure equality of influence, whereas it can
readily be understood that their adoption
involves extinction of minorities —a very grave
evil, and probably the one which would chiefly
bring about an early amendment of this law if
passed as the Assembly made it. Similarly,
the abolition of any money deposit by candi-
dates and the holding of elections on one day,
although presenting a superficial appearance of
favouring the poorer class of candidate, are
likely to operate with killing efficiency in a
contrary direction. A well-to-do man, desirous
of election to the Legislature, can prepare
the way, by keeping himself prominently
before the public for years previously,
and by judicious expenditure in charities
at complimentary banquets and so forth, lu
default of a money deposit every stump-orator
will offer himself as a “ Labour- candidate ’
and carry a few score, or some hundreds, of
votes from the regularly recognised choice of
organised labour. While the popular vote is
thus split, the notable man of the well-to-do
order will have no more competitors of his own
sort than before the deposit was abolished, and
securing a concentration of votes he will win
the seat. So, likewise, as regards all the elec-
tions taking place on one day. In a territory
peopled as is New South Wales, with a number
of concentrated groups of population, an over-
grown metropolis, and some very sparsely
peopled tracts, to compel the denser electorates
to wait for the distant and thinly settled ones,
will be to magnify the chances of the candi-
dates with the fullest purses. The democratic
element in the New South Wales Assembly has,
in framing this bill, “meant wellbut 1 hold
that “ it didn’t know.” —W.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Exit Griffilwraith.
Dear Bulletin,— The Griffilwraith dragon
is dead, but not ere it had devoured Queens-
land. Its manner of death was characteristic
of its whole destructive career. Its vertebra
hung together until Griffith clutched (lie
newly-gilded Chief Justiceship, and until
MTlwraith had made some of his syndicates
secure. The hollow insincerity of public pro-
fessions was never made more clearly visible
than in the Griffith farewell to Parliament.
“ I do not know that Sir Samuel Griffith has
one single enemy in the House.” said H. M.
Nelson, leader of the Opposition. It would
have been nearer the truth, and very near to
the real truth, to have said that Griffith has
not a real friend among the politicians who fol-
lowed him. Friendship only follows trust, and
no sane politician ever trusted Sam Griffith.
It must have been a spectacle for all the devils
of hypocrisy to have seen and heard Morehead
weeping over Griffith, and declaring how
“bitterly” he felt the parting. Morehead’s
bitterness must arise from the fact that he is
losing his favourite.victim of attack, and that
all Queensland politics appear to be dissolving
like the kaleidoscopic views of a chro-
matic design. Griffith owes his suc-
cess to his bowellessness, his celd-blooded-
ness, his intense isolation from all social
sympathies, the whisky proofness of his head,
his capacity for work, that bony and wiry lean
frame of his being equal to any calls made upon
it, his political unscrupulousness, his undoubted
legal ability in a sphere where legal knowledge
and ability tell, and his unshrinking acceptance
of credit for possessing all the “great and
good ” qualities that are supposed to belong to
the cold-blooded son of a “great and good”
(narrow, zealous, and bigoted) parson. If
Griffith dropped down dead to-morrow, all
Brisbane would go into formal mourning—
because Society is an habitual hypocrite—but,
outside his own family, nobody who could
afford it would eat one dinner the less. Summed
up, Griffith is a clever and heartless machine,
constructed to do admirable work where no
feelings of sympathy are required. He cannot
even hate. He can only simulate even that
master-passion of human nature. His main-
spring is ambition, and it is probable he is dis-
contented at heart because of human ingratitude
to him, though he knows no gratitude himself.
Some people get their biographies written in
their life-times, and this is a case in point. As
for MTlwraith, he is simply a syndicator—a
“ hero of civilisation.” He believes—and he is
honest so far that he does believe it —that vic-
tory is for the cunning and strong, and that the
spoils go to the victor. He has no fads about
human equality of opportunity. Show him
schemes for making money, and his only desire
is to lie first in the swim. He is Scotch,
choleric, and apoplectic, but he has enough
desire for life to have “ regulated ” his whusky
lately. He broke with Morehead after a trip
to Japan. He now goes to India. Has the
East another evil thing in store for him ?—H.O.
Reid Cuts a Tooth.
George Houston Reid, interval Leader of the
New South Wales Opposition, has climbed
down. Not that he ever climbed up to his
leadership. He was merely pushed forward by
dismayed malcontents at a time when no one
else cared to accept inconvenient responsibility.
However, being there, he has acquired import-
ance, and, becoming the exponential integer of
a party, it was found necessary to present
always his clean cheek to be kissed. His
previous attitude against Australian Federation
was obnoxious to the loosely-held party behind
him ; for politics are full of accidents, and even
a Reid might become a Premier under the fire-
works rule of Party Government. Reid’s con-
tinuance, then, as Leader of the Opposition,
depended upon his public conversion to Aus-
tralian Federation. The difficulty was to
invent a plausible excuse for an epileptical
change of front. Reid has on several occasions
consistently approached the feat apologetically
and from various angles. But his efforts were
never a success. At last the excuse has come.
Cleveland is evidently going to be elected Presi-
dent of the United States ; therefore, George
Houston Reid is an Australian Federationist.
The sequence is comical, but in keeping with
the freetrade hysterics of jubilation in Sydney
and Melbourne. The daily freetrade press
champs away at its editorials as if it were
already a proclaimed fact that the United
States had become a seaboard—two seaboards,
in fact —of free ports. “ Behold the Eclipse !”
cries the freetrade press, pointing to the
dazzling noonday sun; “let us go ballooning
in a coal-mine.” And it is done. Cleveland
is to be President; therefore, Reid is a
Federationist. The Emperor of Morocco has
lost his umbrella ! therefore, Freetrade is as
dead as last year’s yesterday. Q.E.D .
Something’ Wrong with the Works.
Members of the New South Wales Works
Committee work exceedingly hard, and have a
very difficult task which they grapple in the
most energetic manner. It requires the most
anxious care and most strenuous exertion oa
their part to find themselves something to do.
There is no prospect of any loan funds being
available for a single work which is not already
in course of construction, but the Committee
keeps on sitting with a persistence and deter-
mination not to leave off, which would be
creditable to a broody hen. They have already
inquired into, and despatched sectional com-
mittees (with two guineas per day travelling
expenses) to investigate, about a million sterling
worth of works which can’t possibly be under-
taken until fresh batches of loans are floated.
And as it becomes daily more difficult to find
anything for them to inquire about, some of the
members have hit upon an ingenious plan for
preventing the whole thing coming to
an untimely end. They develope an insatiable
hunger for information, and make one witness
go as far as half-a-dozen used to, in earlier
times. With proper management one witness
can be stretched out to cover an entire sitting.
First the Chairman asks him questions. Then
one member invents as many more as he can,
and when his ingenuity fails there is a third
member ready with a fresh batch of interroga-
tions. Should the imaginations of these in-
dustrious members give out, and no more new
questions occur to any of the Committee, some
member of it steps in to the rescue and asks
the old ones all over again. In this way the
stock of witnesses is economized and the duties
of the Committee will last out this Parliament
anyhow. It used.to interest people at the Colo-
nial Secretary’s office to see one committeeman
brought day after day by his wife in a cab from
a bed of sickness, crawl feebly up the stairs,
drop into his chair exhausted, and earn his
three guineas. The whole business is attended
with a lot of queer work. One member of the
committee, who is far from esteemed in the
Chamber of which he is a member, managed to
get himself elected without difficulty by men
who would have preferred to elect the Soudan
goat. His plan was to corner his fellow
members separately, complain plaintively that
the others had such an unjustifiable grudge
against him that they wouldn’t give him a vote
for a position which was of considerable conse-
quence to him, and express his assurance that
the member he was addressing was not
that sort of a man and would oblige him with
his support. Then the member solicited would
conclude that one vote for the fellow couldn’t
elect him, and would promise him a vote rather
than face the embarrassment of refusing. The
old hand worked this with so many members
that enough voted for him, each believing he
was giving the man the only vote he’d record,
to elect him handsomely. And now he goes
round intimating in his sleek way that the
number of votes he received is a gratifying
proof of the respect and consideration in which
he is Held by his peers.
The “Star” on Socialism.
Says Sydney Star ;
The socialist who takes to the stump and posts as the
friend and saviour of the labouring classes belkvcs in
equality of distribution, but not in equality of produc-
tion.
Now, this is either a gross mis-statement or an
exhibition of limitless ignorance. No Socialist
who understands his subject advocates absolute
equality of distribution ; Avhat socialists demand
is a nearer approach to it than now prevails,
and they desire to make it impossible for one
man, who performs no useful office, to receive
£lOOO daily, while another, sweated out of
human likeness, earns 1000 pence per annum.
Socialism, therefore, does not ask for equality
of distribution but for fairer distribution, and,
knowing “ equality of production ” to be impos-
sible with unequal abilities is, unlike the Star,
not idiotic enough to believe it a corollary of
an approacli to equality in distribution. The
equality that the Socialist demands is neither
of result or share, but of class, circumstance,
effort, and opportunity ; that equality in social,
political, industrial, educational, and judicial
matters that mankind has always been unjustly
denied for selfishness sake.

Ever since four—only four—working men were
appointed to the Maoriland Council the demand
for elective Upper Houses has begun to rage in
the Tory press of Australia. So long as the
Tory party felt absolutely certain that every
Australasian Cabinet would continue to fill its
nominee Council with broken-down political
hacks, money-lenders, bankers, auctioneers, land-
agents, land-boomers, prosperous publicans,
and solid, corpulent employers generally, it
stood like a rock for the old nominee principle.
But now the horrible truth has begun to dawn
on that party that a Cabinet may arise, now
and then, which will actually nominate a few
democrats to the Council, and an exceeding bitter
cry goes forth that Upper Houses ought to he
elected—on a property qualification. The bogus
Liberals of the country will doubtless support
this proposition on the pretence that any kind
of an elective Council will be better than the
present variety—that it will be getting in the
thin edge of the old, blessed, stereotyped
wedge—and that a Council elected on a pro-
perty qualification will be “a step in the right
direction,” and will ultimately. lead to man-
hood suffrage all round. In reality, it will do
nothing of the sort. A Council elected on a
property-qualification will always be strictly
Tory ; it will be stronger (as witness
the experience of Victoria) in the cause
of property than the nominee body, because it
will have its constituents behind it as a sup-
port, whereas the nominee Council has no sup-
port at all; it cannot be swamped by a
democratic Cabinet: and it cannot be reformed
save with its own consent, and its constituents
will help it to resist reform. An Upper House,
elected on a property basis, is an institution
without one redeeming feature, and will sound
the death-knell of democratic progress.
The Shiels Government (Vic.) is quietly stone-
walling the bill for the repeal of the so called
Voluntary Liquidation Act, which measure
plainly has no earthly chance of passing this
session. The Voluntary Liquidation Act, or,
more correctly, the Act to prevent liquidation
and cover up the frauds perpetrated by “repre-
sentative citizens,” is one of the most remarkable
measures ever sneaked through the Legislature
by a broken-down banker and his accomplices.
Under its provisions the busted companies,
which are now being quietly wound-up without
any unpleasant scandal or exposure, can’t be
placed under the supervision of the court unless
by the decision of a majority of the creditors.
The advantages of this arrangement are beauti-
fully obvious. At the original liquidation
meeting the boss director probably took the
chair, and being armed with a barrow-load of
proxies presumably genuine from alleged
creditors, many of whom only claimed an odd
shilling or so as the balance of a current
account, he promptly secured the appoint-
ment of the same old gang of directors or
their friends to do the winding-up. Then
these individuals peacefully set to work to
smother their own swindles, and write off their
own overdrafts, and report that their own
bogus securities were all right, and their own
doings all perfectly honest. And now this
arrangement can’t he overset in the majority of
the cases because it requires the votes of more
than 50 per cent, of the creditors to do it, and
more than the required 50 per cent, are
generally absentees, or dead men, or persons
who can’t be found, or persons who never could
be found, with old balances of a shadowy
amount standing to their credit. The genuine
creditors can’t prove these individuals to be
bogus, and thus the liquidators, with their
dead men’s votes, are masters of the situation.
A good many “ banks,” where there would
otherwise be much trouble, are being wound up
peacefully under this blessed system ; and the
Shires Cabinet, which acts as if it were the
pimp and flunkey of the good, old, representa-
tive gang, though it hasn’t the courage to
openly oppose its abolition, is gently, but
effectively blocking the Bill which has been
introduced for that purpose. It doesn’t commit
itself, but every time the Bill comes up, somc-
thing gets in the way, apparently by accident.
The sinful absurdity of the “ fine or imprison-
ment ” system w'as vividly illustrated at Rich-
mond (Melb.) police court, the other day.
Several larrikins were found guilty in various
degrees of brutally ill-treating a woman, and
whilst one of them got sent up for a month
certain, another, considered to be less culpable,
was sentenced to “ £lO, or three months.” This
by a law which professes to make no distinc-
tion between the stonebroker and the million-
aire. Presuming that in the latter, case the
culprit couldn’t raise a “tenner,” or that he
possessed the virtue of Thrift and couldn’t bring
himself to cash up the sum required, he is
serving three times the term of imprisonment
which will purge the greater offender of his
guilt. Looked at from a purely financial point
of view the cost of incarceration in a dungeon
is here fixed at £3 6s. Bd. per month, and the
wealthy bank swindler, who might easily write
a cheque for a hundred years of penal servitude,
has a right to consider himself aggrieved when
he doesn’t get the option.
Tadema and Poynter have received £2OOO
for painting panels on a piano for an American
millionaire.
The late Deputy-Master Hunt (Sydney
Mint) distinctly told a Bulletin man that no
power existed to coin silver at that “branch of the
Royal Mint.” A new Deputy-Master reigns, and
he (according to Premier Dibbs) has apparently
discovered that “authority already exists under
the Order-in-Council ” that authorised the Mint
to perform the work of gold coinage. Now, there
is only one “ Royal Mint ” of England, and only
one “Master of the Mint.” Any outside mints,
such as those of Sydney or Melbourne, have no
official status beyond that of “ Branch of the
Royal Mint,” and their “ Master ” is only
“ Deputy Master.” The “ Annual Reports of the
Deputy Master of Sydney Branch of the Royal
Mint ” (the late Mr. Hunt assured the writer) are
required to be furnished direct to the Royal Mint,
London, and not until they have been received
they be furnished to the Government
and Parliament here ! Indeed, the writer is not
quite sure that the boundary-line of the late Mr.
Hunt’s statement was not this —that he sent his
annual reports to London, and that it was London
which sent them on, as a matter of routine
courtesy, to the N.S. Wales Government.
The latest addition to N.S. W. National
Gallery, Poynter’s “Queen of Sheba visiting King
Solomon,” is not only the largest and most ambi-
tious canvas on the walls, but sad to relate, it has
also cost more money than anything that has been
purchased up to the present time. The sum paid
for it was >£2900, and considering that it was
originally bought, only some two or three years
ago, for .£6OOO, it would seem at first glance that
the trustees have made a remarkably good bargain.
This, however, is hardly the proper light in which
to regard the transaction. A picture whose
market-value has deteriorated in so short a period
to the extent of more than 50 per cent, is scarcely
likely to increase in value. The truth is that
Poynter’s art is, and always has been, of a purely
conventional type, and as the tendency of modern
taste is to revolt against conventionalism the
chances are that the artist and the school to which
he belongs will, before long, be either forgotten or
despised, except for the technical merit of
the productions. This picture is unquestionably
the painter’s masterpiece, and shows to the full
his knowledge of the period he is illustrating.
Thoroughness was ever Poynter’s motto, and
nothing could exceed the conscientious care with
which he has studied every possible detail and the
consummate skill he has shown in representing
them on the canvas. Take, for example, the
management of the bright sunlight finding its way
from behind the canopy over the throne and
striking the red pillars in the background, than
which nothing could be more masterly in treat-
ment and realistic in effect. Indeed, as a mere
piece of workmanship it would be impossible to
praise the picture too highly. But something
beyond the ability to paint and the possession of a
deep knowledge of archaeological lore is required
for the production of a work that can truly be
called great, and that something is certainly lack-
ing here. Neither the figure of Solomon nor that
of the Queen possesses any dignity, and they sim-
ply strike the spectator as ordinary models,
dressed up in gorgeous attire for the purpose of
being painted in somewhat constrained attitudes,
while none of the numerous crowd loafing
round the throne appear to take any interest in
the proceedings. The one touch of nature in the
whole picture is the graceful girl in the foreground,
poised on her left foot and turning towards a
monkey she is leading by a string. When it is
taken into consideration that in the Sydney
Gallery there are no examples of such painters of
the English school as Alma Tadema, Frederick
Walker, Dante Rossetti, and Burne-Jones, all of
whom are greater artists than Poynter in the
highest sense of the term, it certainly seems that
the =£2900 might have been more wisely expended
than in the purchase of a picture, the sole interest
of which consists in the accessories. It may be
mentioned that the advent of this new arrival has
necessitated a re-arrangement of the gallery with
the result that the Poynter is flanked on one side
by Topham’s vulgarly-painted and ill-drawn
“Savonarola ” and on the other by Long’s trivial
and inept “Dorcas Meeting,” so that whatever
benefit there was to be derived by contrast has
been accorded to it.
BALLOT[?]. BULLET.
The battle’s near, each beacon bright
Has flashed the news o’er land and sea;
Be wakeful now, ye ranks of Right 1
Ye need no arms, the coming fight
Will prove a bloodless victory.
The Day is near, tho’ yet Night shrouds
The fertile earth it held so long.
Awake ! Arise ! Ye slumb’ring crowds !
Your voice can break the jealous clouds
That veil the laggard light of Wrong
Unite your votes ! Your foes will fall
Before your numbers in the fray ;
No glitt’ring steel, no “ pinging ” ball,
No beat of drum, no bugle call
Will usher in the long’d-for Day.
Unite 1 for you the sabre's sheath
Holds naught to cope with stolid Law ;
Be true ! The Fates your laurels wreathe ;
Vob Victis ! To your sons bequeath
All gifts divine as spoils of war.
