My Dear Moorabinda,—
On dit that the reason why a certain Governor’s wife
won’t open the frequent exhibitions of a certain society
(not to put too fine a point upon it) is that she never
knows what’s going to happen to her when she gets there.
The first time she went, a piece of music, composed
expressly for her, was performed, and when it was over
somebody asked if the composer might be presented to
her ladyship. She graciously assented, but when Wagner
was led up by the ear he was hopelessly tipsy ! His beefy
bow halt an inch off her ladyship’s nose gave him away in
one posture, and his reply in response to her Ex.’s well-
turned compliments wasn’t intelligible enough to be
phonographed. That’s vice-regal experience No. 1.
Experience No. 2 is less vulgar, but more aesthetically
painful, perhaps. The society in question is a mixed
aggregate, and as her ladyship was being escorted round
the show (on her second visit) by all the presentable com-
mittee-men, an utterly impossible member burst through
the cordon and hurled himself at the Governor’s wife.
He was effusive and aitchless, but it was at his dressing
that Her Ex. drew the line. Large-check pants, a pepper-
and-salt coat, no waistcoat, and a tweed cap, is hardly
the sort of get-up you’d order before escorting a
Governor’s wife round a show, and yet this thunder-and-
lightning party hung at the vice-regal elbow during the
whole parade, while the half-crown audience stood aghast.
And now for the most horrifying incident of the lot.
Her Ex. has just arrived at the show, and was being
received by the committee when a fat and eccentric
member stepped forth and actually chucked her under
the chin! “ You’re looking cheerful, me lady," he said
encouragingly, “ but you’re too pale—we wanter see more
roses in yer cheeks”—but his poetic flow was out off
short at the meter by the justly-affronted patroness rush-
ing past him speechless with indignation. She gave up
the society in despair after that, and when the next
deputation called at Government House to bespeak her
patronage they were received by a frozen aide-de-camp,
who remarked in a fog-like manner that her ladyship was
too—er—nervous to patronize a show where the unex-
pected almost always happens (or words to that effect) ;
and now the exhibitions are opened by all sorts of
illustrious odds and ends who don’t dream of the pitfalls
they’re risking.
The Sydney citizens’ return oall last week to Mayor and
Mayoress Manning was the social failure of the season—
from the prononcd decorations to the nutshell crowd.
And no wonder, for there was precious little sympathy
with it. It is part of the “ I take but I surrender” game
to give the City at least one big headache in the Town
Hall during the year, and the City doesn’t feel inclined to
rise frantically on its hind-legs and trump the purely
official trick. And it strikes me as being indelicate, too.
In society, the social I O U is redeemed in a lighter manner
than by flatly “returning” this or that festivity—just
as one old woman hands a gridiron over the back-
fence to the other old woman who lent her a tub.
On the principle of one for you and two for myself, a
return ball to a mayor or anybody else is no more to be
objected to than any other outbreak where a handful
of men resolve themselves into a committee, and
cadge a few pot plants and a flag or two, and announce
the tickets one guinea. Because the idea is really to
“ fill ’em up agin,” in a manner of speaking—to dovetail
another barn-darnoe into the order of existing things.
Besides which, there are men who pine and wither unless
there is a prospect ahead of perspiring behind a big
blue rosette and gyrating in the middle of a waxed floor
making jump-up signals with a spotless handkerchief for
the band to play “God Save ” on the “approach of the
Guvnah.” This gentleman (of course not the Governor)
is always spying out leaks in the social armour which can
only be properly plugged with a ball, and the result is—
well_that he has his inopportune moments.
“ Fools rush in,” &c., comes into your head when you
think of giving a banquet (with Burdekin on the arrange-
ment committee) to Lord Charles Precedence Scott. It was
decidedly a risky game. As Sydney once narrowly escaped
ceasing to be Australia’s chief naval station because the
scion of the house of Buccleuch was placed at table on
the off side of the Governor of the neighboring province,
if would have been in better taste had they hit on a
festivity less fraught with disastrous consequences in the
past. Also, Lord Charles was understood to remark on
the momentous occasion that “ he would never aga’n
at tend another public dinner in Sydney ’’—but, I suppose)
that doesn’t oount. When a man says he will never go to
another funeral ha doesn’t mean that he isn’t going to his
own.
The daily papers have leaders now on the question of
female Toby Tosspots. The discussion all arises from the
fact that a certain titled Englishwoman said, the other
day, that piok-me-ups and “ nips ” and “ pegs ” and
“little goes” were quite boudoir refreshers nowadays,
and that women openly drank champagne-cup without
any hesitation. All of which can go for gospel. In
Sydney, haven’t we swell bathing-rooms where ladies go
ostensibly to have a wash, hut where the faintest wink at
the attendant procures a “reviver” of masculine
malignity? There’s a terrible amount of “boozing”-
good old Shakesperian word, I assure you—nowadays,
and some of the results are tragic.
One could pile up instances of pleasant homes going
under the hammer, and of children farmed out all round
because of a wife’s intemperance. The latest society
instance is a lady—(yet)—a charming woman, too, with
several cherubic children and a devoted husband. Her
case is in the doctor’s hands, and he advised taking her
away from her cronies—every drunken woman has a lot
of drunken hags of “ friends ”—as the last hope. So her
husband started her out on a long sea-voyage, idiotically
enough, I think, for there is every temptation and oppor-
tunity to give way on a ship where people go about pre-
scribing all sorts of nips as infallible remedies for mal-de-
mer. And if she travels in the ladies’ cabin she’s lost.
I’ll never forget a woman I left in a ladies’ cabin once.
She had an empty beer-bottle on either side of her and one
at her feet, and she was howling for brandy-and-soda as if
her life depended on it.
The enormous catalogue of John Harrises who turned
up in the lawsuit at Sydney over the will of a gilded
defunct of that name points to the moral that a tribe can
overdo calling every boy-baby that comes along by the
name of its rich uncle in the hope that he will get a fat
bequest. It often only leads to the lawyers getting the
whole oyster.
Lady Jersey has commenced “impressioning ” about
the “ Australian Colonies” in an English monthly, but,
judging from cabled scraps, this article is simply sop
compared to the yarns which, I’ll venture to wager,
M.E. J. will spin about us when her foot is on her native
heath. Lady J. will yet turn out livelier copy on Aus-
tralia than this article, I’m sure. Where is the woman who
wouldn’t let herself go on the subject of a land where the
only redeeming feature was that nobody copied her
bonnets ?
At the return ball to the Mayor of Sydney the other
evening the fun in the gentlemen’s cloak-room consisted
in fitting on Sir Henry Parkes’ hat. As Sir Henry takes
an out size in head-coverings it obliterated most of ’em as
effectually as an extinguisher.
The objections raised in Melbourne to the tugs-of-war
between teams of women strike one as being most ridi-
culous considering how long this sort of thing has been
going on. There always have been feminine war-tugs.
For instance, when Mrs. de Smythe enters a room with a
new frock on and sees Mrs. de Jones there already in a
newer—there’s one then.
Madame Weigal’s fashion journal for November is out.
It shows nothing new, except that some of the young
ladies reveal a gooddealofcollar-bonetor street-work, and
some of the bonnets seem to argue that Australian
belledom’s skull is as sun-proof as the woolly pate of
King Billy. Waists are still kept down to indigestion
pitch, and the Watteau bow—having descended to the
cheap Jacks—doesn’t figure any more in the plates of
Wei gal.
The alleged defrauding of a Sydney jewellery firm by
“Colonel Andrew Burney” was a serious matter, no
doubt, but still the victim got a “ displayed ” advertise-
ment out of it: “Witness showed the accused a gold
chronograph saying that it was the same as one worn by
Lord Jersey.” And now we all know where Gov. Jersey
got his watch.
But the jeweller almost deserved to be taken down, for
his deep and placid trust in everything that came out of
Government House. He parted with the jewellery
“entirely on the representation that accused was stopping
at Government House ! ” Imagine what a holy of holies
the Governor’s residence must be when a tradesman
cheerfully hands over nearly £lOO worth of rings and
things just because a well-dressed man throws down an
envelope with the vice-regal crest on it and takes the
lord’s name in vain !
The pupils of Sydney Technical College cooking-class
are “disaffected,” because the two lady examiners of
their work have been replaced by two doctors and the
chef at Parliament House, and only an “insufficient
reason ” given for the change. B it, to my way of think-
ing, “set a man to judge the meat ” ought to be a pro-
verb. Most of the cookery in the world is done for man
the hungry animal who devours right down the tndnu in
public places, while woman diffidently orders a cup of
tea and a bun. Women don’t care for an elaborate set-
out, though I think they are more snack-hungry than
men—they require more cups of tea and spasmodic pastry
hut, judging by the way they choose an ice-cream in
preference to a prime cut, I should say man was far and
away the more competent as a judge of cookery. So long
as the aspirant for culinary laurels puts a heavy frost of
pink icing on the top and a great deal of paper-collar
round her pie, the lady judges wouldn’t be able to contain
their raptures, though the competitors’ plain chop was
raw in the middle.
The Bananaland youth is far indeed from the fold of
social culture. lam told that a large number of him
never by any chance speak of a lady as a lady, but call
her, bluntly and inelegantly, a “gin.” At a* dance, if
you ask him who is his partner, ten to one he tells you
“ that gin in the green dress over there and even when
he is engaged he proudly points out his lady-love as
“There’s my gin!” The only way the Queensland girl
can get square is by calling him “ King Billy.”
I hope the N.S.W. Animals Protection society won’t let
the idea of a horse-and-cab parade in Sydney Domain fall
through. Everybody likes to ride in a smart hansom
with a sleek horse instead of being bumped along in a
broken-windowed sarcophagus behind amass of hair and
fatigue; and by giving prizes to the best turn-out you
minimise your chance of emerging from a cab like
Ophelia, with straws in your hair. lam sure a house-to-
house collection would soon raise enough money to pay
all expenses of a parade—and if they could only get the
conspicuously liberal Bishop Smith to hand the" prizes
to the successful cabbies there would be nothing left to
wish for.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

When John Gourlay is in doubt he in-
variably plays “Skipped by the Light of the
Moon,” but it begins to be an open question
whether the Sydney public won’t get tired of that
vociferous trifle when it has been put on seven
or eight hundred times more. Even the dog'
which devastates the red - haired Y.M.C.A.
person, and tears most Of the clothes off
that mangled hypocrite, palls after a while.
Apart from the fact that Mr. Gourlay seems to
throw his dishevelled personality up in the air
more wildly at each successive revival, there is
nothing new to report. The agile John and his
partner, Mr. Walton, fill the parts of the two
Y.M.C.A. youths with eclat. Mr. W. Warner,
with a bad cold, does the heavy policeman that is
afflicted with the iron tank-intellect. Mr. G.
Lash Gordon, as a mahogany-headed detective,
presents sundry commendable features ; and that
pretty little lady, Miss Amy Gourlay—about the
only person in the bill who is allowed by the
author to conduct herself as if she wasn’t off her
glossy little head —is a bright oasis in a desert of
frenzy, and a shining light against a background
of cast-iron mirth.
** * *
Fillis’ Circus, at Sydney Carrington
Grounds, seems pretty well up .to its promises.
The trained elephant and educated monkey ele-
ment is tolerably strong in this show, as it is in
every right-thinking circus, and the pony-race
with apes as jockeys appeals to the true inward-
ness of an Australian audience. The lion-taming
act is even more thrilling than usual, for the four
animals are all of a bitter and sulky disposition,
and their desire to dine on the lion-tamer is
obvious from the very beginning of the perform-
ance. Up to date that scarred veteran has dodged
his fate successfully, but his end is assuredly
approaching. The prettiest feature in the pro-
gramme, however, is the tank performance—a
bright and original item which lacks the horrible,
raw-beef significance that attaches to the lion
business. Professor Beaumont and two airy, fish-
like maidens hold a series of revels under water in
this act, and gambol around at the bottom of the
glass cistern like a frog’s birthday celebration.
Then the Professor throws quantities of
loose silver into the tank, and dives down
with his hands tied behind his back, and proceeds
to collect it in his mouth. It is a weird sight—
this aquatic gentleman, with the limelight on him,
feet upwards in the water, and gathering small
change with his head ; and when he has collected
it all, and, rising leisurely to the surface, spits
£2 17s. 3d. into a plate, the applause is tremen-
dous. After that he descends with about 501bs.
of shot in his arms, and lying down at the bottom
of the tank goes off to sleep—apparently. He is
reputed to have stayed at the bottom, with the
shot on his manly chest, for something over
minutes ; on Saturday, so far as the excited
audience could keep the record with its watch, he
remained under for minutes. This aquatic
performance is the great success of the show, and
if Fillis wants to make the biggest fortune on
record, he should turn the whole arena into a
tank, and run the entire exhibition under water.
** * *
Sydney, at the present time, just manages
to support two theatres and a few side-shows and
cycloramas in reasonable comfort, but what will
happen when the Royal, Criterion, and Lyceum
get into working order grows more and more
problematical. There is no money in the poor,
dejected city, and unless the management makes
a new departure, and, failing hard cash, takes
deposit-receipts and bank shares at the door, it is
difficult to imagine what will happen. Legally-
executed assignments of debts due by building
societies, equities of redemption, debentures, and
second mortgages might also be accepted. Other-
wise, one at least of the new theatres seems likely
to become a doss house for the unemployed, or else
a soup-kitchen.
** * *
“The English Rose,” with still another
member of the Rignold family—Lionel, cousin of
George and his brother Biil—in the lead, will take
the stage at Her Majesty’s (Sydney) on Saturday.
Lionel is a newly-imported gentleman from King-
land, understood to be a thing of considerable
beauty and a personal ornament. A strong co.
has been organised to support him, and “ the
celebrated horse, Bushman,” has been engaged for
the steeplechase scene.
** * *
The veteran James Hasker, aged 74, is to
have a benefit at Sydney Royal Standard on Mon-
day (28th Nov.). The theatrical managers of the
city, with the single exception, so we are informed,
of Mr. John Gourlay, who gave his support on a
generous scale, appear to have looked somewhat
coldly on the old Australian actor, and he has had
to fall back on the assistance of the Government
Printing-office Dramatic Society. A benefit in an
obscure theatre with the aid of an amateur co., no
matter how capable, is not the send-off which a
man of Mr. Masker’s years and reputation de-
serves, especially considering the huge benefits
which are bestowed so lavishly on the imported
mummer. Still, The Bulletin trusts and believes
that the show will realise a handsome profit for
the venerable artist.
** * *
Adelaide Royal was shut for a week to
allow of fumigation, after Rickards left it, and it
re-opened on Saturday (Nov. 19) with the Green-
woods in “My Sweetheart.” The sisters Albu
began a concert-season at the Town Hall on the
same night. At Garner’s, Bateman and O’Grady,
helped by F. C. Appleton and others, have been
playing “Current Cash,” but there hasn’t been
much cash current. On Saturday they changed to
the “ Wearing of the Green.”
&* * *
“Joan of Arc,” at Melbourne Princess’s,
had its career prolonged over Saturday, owing to
the sudden indisposition of Marion Hood, a fair
vessel who seems apt to become “ laid up ” at criti-
cal moments. On the Friday night, a medium-sized
substitute appeared unexpectedly amidst the folds
of the original amazon’s armour, and meandered
through the name-part without drawing much
attention to the other lady’s absence. But when
the time came for changing the bill, Marion was
still unable to show, therefore an extra perform-
ance of “Joan of Arc” took place, whilst “Miss
Esmeralda” was announced to happen on Monday
last. The enrollment of Clara Merivale as a
member of the Gaiety Co. should strengthen
matters rather than otherwise, so “ Miss Esmer-
alda ” is doubtless another unparalleled success.
Marion’s sickness may even turn out to be
a melancholy blessing in disguise, but no
matter. The very last night of “ Joan ” acquired
poetic interest from the fainting away of Alice
Lethbridge, during a pas de deux with Lonnen.
As the comedian picked up his partner and bore
the lovely burden away, a suspicion that Alice
might have died in the discharge of her duty ran
through the house. Indeed, a johnnie in the
reserved stalls was about to choke himself with his
opera-glasses when the performance went on as
usual and public confidence was restored. Subse-
quently Alice came forth once more to get such a
reception as may perhaps encourage her to reel
and fall gracefully on some future occasion.
Meanwhile it is satisfactory to know that the
plucky young creature, who always whirls regard-
less of the weather, didn’t seem to have suffered
by contact with the floor. Happy boards !
** * *
Sassiety has been giving generous support
to “Niobe,” at the Bijou, partly because the
comedy is funny out of the ordinary way, and
also because the honourable reign of B. and B.
draws rapidly to a close. Even the gallery is
learning to see some good points in Niobe, not-
withstanding her unpopularity with those gods of
yore, who, as Brough explains to the astonished
Greek lady, “have had to knock under.” On
Tuesday last Mrs. Brough benefited in “ The
Squire’’and portion of “Dandy Dick,” but the
old bill was to be renewed on the following night
and will run until Saturday, when Grundy’s play,
“ A White Lie,” takes the stage.
* * *
The other three Melbourne theatres are
not of much consequence just now. After one
night of “The Stolen Goddess” the Alexandra
shut up more completely than the old Royal, inas-
much as the Royal is hired for an occasional per-
formance. The Alabama Minstrels are in the
throes of a final week at the Opera House, and
nobody talks of filling their place. Also Max
O’Rell has folded up his lectures and gone silently
away from the boomed city, and the Gaiety is
taking a rest until Saturday.
** * *
St. George’s Ha'l still struggles along
under the management of Coghill Bros., however,
and the Waxworks have been visited by the
wonderful talking cockatoo, a bird of such rare
intelligence and sensibility that somebody had to
shut out its view of the wax figures before it could
be induced to speak at all. They suggested death,
and in the presence of a corpse the cockatoo felt
bound to maintain a respectful silence.
* 4k * *
The Melbourne Exhibition Building was
recently the scene of a ghastly mockery described
as a Ladies’ Tug-of-war. A few big Women,
assisted by a number of small girls, engaged in
spasmodic pulls at a rope. Sometimes they pulled,
but more often they were drying their delicate
hands on the upper portions of their garments, and
altogether they were a plenteous source of woe to
sundry charitable associations of the softer sex.
Moreover, they proved a financial failure—
Speaker Bent, the two Houses of Parliament,
several bookmakers, and a contingent of lecherous
Chinamen being the only respectable patrons of
the entertainment.
** * *
Preparations for a different class of exhi-
bition in the same building are being pushed
along. A Santa Claus Festival is getting ready
for Christmas, and the musical features of this
new enterprise promise to catch on. Some of the
people now singing at the Saturday concerts will
burst forth in operettas, and of afternoons there
will be a performance of Julien’s tricky British
Army Quadrilles, which consists largely of brass
bands marching up from the distance to the strains
of warlike music and finishing up with “ Rule
Britannia,” or something, on the platform. As a
musical illustration of h’Old Hingland’s glory this
masterpiece always achieves a dreadful triumph,
** * *
Carington, as the new Lord Chamberlain,
promises to be a gorgeous joke. Although the
public knowledge of these functionaries is con-
fined to the fact that they are nominal licensers
of plays (the Queen alone knows what other duties
they dally with), it has been usual for the L.C. to
leave the care of the drama to somebody else. Of
late years, a Mr. Pigott has been the actual
play licenser. Carington, however, promptly
seized the idea of personally looking after the
interests of British morality. He notified the
London managers that he proposed to be present
when they made their next application for the
renewal of their theatre licenses, so that he might
form their acquaintance, and enquire after the
Lotties and the Totties in a strictly official
manner. Also, he intends to read all the plays
that are suspected of being improper, with a view
to seeing whether they make him blush. If
they don’t make him blush, Carington will
pass them, so ’Ome stands a chance of getting
some piping-hot comedies shortly. The question
of ballet-girls’ skirts being a specially important
one just now, Carington has privately announced
his desire to inspect all ballet-girls respecting
whose wardrobe there is any possible shadow of
doubt whatever. Furthermore, he is understood
to be considering the advisability of putting lead-
ing ladies through their Catechism, in order to
find out whether they are as good as they should
be, and if they fall short of his ideal they will
have to sit in the Lord Chamberlain’s office and
grow better. Altogether the late Gov. of N.S. W.
promises to bring an ancient billet fairly up to
date. Six years of yawning in Sydney restored
his jaded constitution considerably. The old
Adam has reasserted itself in our friend the Baron,
and he intends to make things hum in that path of
life to which it has pleased God to call him.
Mrs. Beefcon says: “Claret should be
sound, fruity, and soft as satin, and all the com-
ponents happily blended into a homogeneous
whole. Easy of digestion, it is a mildly stimu-
lating drink, in every way fulfilling the Scriptural
character as a ‘ wine that maketh glad the heart
of man.’” Innes’s Boar’s Head Clarets, at all
grocers, at Is., Is. 6d., 25., and 3s. a bottle, can
be recommended as purer and better wines than
French clarets, and they are only one-third of the
price.

At Poverty Point.
“Love rules the camp, the court, the Musgrove."’
“ Yvonne,” who has written the lyrical music for one
or two of the recently-produced comic operas, notably
“Incognita” at London Lyric, is no other than our old
friend, Alfred Moule, who modestly hides his great per-
sonality under that euphonious pen-name.
There will be an interesting performance at Melbourne
Royal on Saturday, the 26th, when Miss Harrie Ireland
appears as the heroine in “ Clytie ” for the benefit of her
veteran papa. Miss Ireland is a handsome, clever girl,
who may, one of these days, be boomed a la Essie
Jenyns.
’Edmund Fisher is “trimming” the Princess panto.
Probably “ the old hand and his gal ” are working on the
Royal one.
Bill Holloway is very chirpy and very much to the fore
in London, and, when the mail left anticipated making
a hit as Kent in “King Lear.” He says, contrary to
general rumour, that he has no intention of returning to
“ the colonies.” He also states that Essie Jenyns’ sister
does not contemplate adopting the stage as a profession.
Hullar, snake-charmer, in Melbourne, from America
and India, revives all the Shires and Underwood business.
He picked up 60 tiger-snakes in the recent Seymour
floods, and is indifferent to bites, just as some people are
proof against arsenic.
Wybert Reeve, as stage-manager of the Melbourne
Bijou, had a very off-hand style of cegaging supers., and
used just to pounce on whoever he could find outside the
Stage-door, in Little Collins-street. The yarn goes that,
wanting a captain of the guard tor the Majeronis’ “ Eliza-
beth,” he impressed a magnificent civil servant who hap-
pened to be passing.
Very nervous was Billy Emerson, at Melb. Opera House,
the other night, introducing a joke about Wales and
baccarat, for he might remember how another American,
Fred Lyster, put his foot in it, and was nigh driven from
the stage by venturing a joke, in a Ballarat hotel, about
the Clontarf business.
Lionel Rignold is accompanied to Australia by his wife,
a clever actress, but they only stop three months.
“Jack Sheppard ” is the regulation play to wind up a
bust management, but the Alexandra people forgot it,
and caved in on a statue play.
A London critic calls him Kyrle Balloon because he is
so puffed up.
Bpong, the Bijou scenic artist, contemplates returning
to England.

Mister James 1
The revised version of “Cinder Ellen,” at London
Gaiety, contains a “ Man in the Moon ” duet, adapted by
Leslie from the old Fortescuaditty.
Marie Halton has been a success in “LaCigale,” at
London Lyric, and threatens to open another London
theatre in February with a new comic opera, founded on
the Peg Woffington story.
Stephen Fiske, an American critic, relates that Mary
Anderson’s first big success was in the “ Lady of Lyons ” ;
and Fiske was so enthusiastic about her that he rushed
round to invite the whole Co. to supper. Then he found
that the new star had gone home. The Co. went after
her, and discovered her sitting on a trunk, eating a
cold pork chop with her fingers, but she promptly
abandoned the chop, and came out with them to the
nearest restaurant, where she supped on candy and raw
oysters. There must have been a good deal of the ostrich
about Mary in those days.
The veteran Wybert Reeve, lessee of Adelaide Royal,
has latterly been in a most melancholy mood. He says
that during the past 18 months he has produced 77
different pieces, ranging from high tragedy with the
Jewess Bernhardt to the very lowest of low comedy with
that excellent Christian Rickards—and yet people aren’t
satisfied. During the Rickards season, indeed, the
receipts got down to £8 one night, and even on the Prince
of Wales’ Birthday they were less than £4O. It costs, if
Wybert is right, more than £5O a night to run the show.
The Italian Operatic Co. smashed finally the other day
in Dunedin. The papers were unfriendly, and made
remarks about the bulky proportions of Signora Cuttica,
and even charged that sweet songstress with having a voice
that was half-way between a steam-whistle and a nutmeg-
grater. The weather was cold, and when an Italian is
cold he is about as unmanageable as a boa-constrictor
that has got frozen. And finally, after much friction
over various small matters, the red-nosed and frost-bitten
company said it would see manager Jinkins darned first,
and declined to sing a note.

Recipe for a Gaiety burlesque: Take a slab of “ Ivan-
hoe,” a bit of the “ Hunchback of Notre Dame,” most of
Joe Miller’s ancient jokes, some scraps of “ Mikado ” and
the “Bohemian Girl,” blow the whole thing up with
dynamite, have the debris struck by lightning, shove it
on the stage backwards with the limelight on it, and then
turn the orchestra on full blast, and call the result
“ Maritana,” or “Moses in Egypt,” or anything.
One Barlow, in the Contemporary, has a blasphemous
article which says that neither Irving nor Ellen Terry can
speak Shakespere at all, and Irving’s is intolerable mouth-
ings. His ideals are M. Piron and Mdlle Mea, two artists
of Bernhardt’s Co., both familiar here.

“ Mr. Shell, the London magistrate, has a strong dash
of the cynic in his constitution. Once he and Mr. Paget
used to sit alternately. Mr. Paget had a case of cruelty
to a horse before him, and left it over to Mr. Sheil to take,
saying, ‘ Mr. Sheil has a horse.’ The next day Mr. Sheil
remitted to his brother magistrate a case of wife-beating,
with the remark, ‘Mr. Paget has a wife.’ ” — Hawk.

Haddon Chambers seeks knowledge at the fountain
head, and as a dramatist looks for his types of character
in real life. He has thus acquired an extensive and
peculiar knowledge of “Greater London” which might
almost be envied by even Walter Besant. While pere-
grinating a few evenings ago, he met a friend, with
whom he sauntered into London Pavilion. As they
entered and took their seats they noticed a little knot
engaged in an animated discussion, which ceased as they
passed. Presently one of the disputants arose with,
“ Mr. Chambers, will you let me interdooce you to my
friend, Jem ’All, a countryman of yours?”—whereupon
Jem seized Haddon’s hand with a grip of iron. “ I’m
proud to meet ye Mr. ’Adding Chambers. D’ye see them
chaps over there? Well, they’ve been arguin’ an’
chyakin’ me till I got riled. I says to them, ‘We can lick
England at scullin’, and we can lick ye at flghtin’, an’
s’eip me, I b’leeve we can lick ye at any think.’ ‘ All
right, says they, we grant ye can do us at scullin’ an’
boxin’, but ye ain’t got no Manky brains!’ An’ jest
then you come in.” “ Look ’ere, you coves,” said Jim,
pointing triumphantly at Haddon, “ what d’ye say to
brains now, eh? Ain’t Mr. ’Adding Chambers got ’em,
eh?” And so, thanks to Jem Hall and Haddon Chambers,
the intellectual repute of Australia was saved.

At Scone (N S.W.) School of Arts The
Bulletin file is secured by a patent padlock.
None of the other paper files are secured. They
are safely anchored by their own weight.
Should his health have improved, Sir
Charles Lilley will probably address a meeting in
Sydney Town Hall, upon his return from Maori-
land. If he does, he will receive from the Sydney
Republicans a welcome recalling that extended to
Grey.
Lilley is the founder of Free Education in
Australia, having written in the Governor’s
speech, in 1869, that all the schools in Queensland
should be free. His efforts to make the grammar-
schools free were defeated by one vote. Two years
afterwards Victoria followed Queensland.
Chief Justice Lilley gave the copy of his
judgment in the big law-case to the B. Telegraph
reporter, with a warning not to let the manuscript
get into the hands of the Courier; and Buzacott
had to wait in humiliation until he received
revised proofs from the TELEGRAPH. This may
have had something to do with the bitterness of
the Courier’s attacks on Lilley.
Queensland having refused to join in the
cable guarantee, London Times ' : has decided to
exclude all Queensland cable messages from its
columns.” So much the worse for the Times.
Every time a Queensland sensation is prominently
dealt with in the cable columns of the live London
dailies, the fast-waning Times prestige will get an
ugly knock. It might as well decide to exclude
the daily quotations of London Stock Exchange.
The announcement only goes to prove on what
insane lines of self-complacency the senile grey-
beard of the press is now being run.
Sir Julius Vogel has been appointed
London manager for the New York Life Assur-
ance Co. Any live company always likes to
get a big, glaring, expensive man for a figurehead,
and Vogel, that hardened borrower of many
millions, is big and glaring and expensive
enough for anything. Unless the auditor keeps
an eye on him he will presently hire the whole
Royal Academy to paint the door of his private
room, and have the carpet bordered with pearls,
and “shout ” gold teeth for the entire staff as an
advertisement. Still he will push business
tremendously, unless the big rival company hires
Wales as a counterblast.

The syndicate press are praising Lords Kintore
and Jersey for “the valuable work ” done towards the
settlement of the Broken Hill strike. Everybody else is
asking what they did, and where the proof of it is to be
found. Perhaps it is through their influence that the
miners have only to crawl back on their hands and knees,
instead of wriggling back on their bellies, and that the
thick-souled directors are not using bigger hob-nails in
the thick-soled boots with which they are vigorously
jumping on the broken unionists. Even this modified
service, however, does not appear to be well authenti-
cated.

* * *
“ Can it be the apathy of despair that causes the
silence, or at least the dullness almost to acquiescence, in
the financial scandals and present wretched state of
affairs?” asks an indignant Geelong man of the Argus.
No, it isn’t the apathy of despair. It’s the influence of
too much meat consumed in an enervating climate, com-
bined with the appalling fact that every third “influen-
tial ” Victorian is using his influence to shut up the man
on either side of him.
# * *
Says a Melbourne paper “It has transpired
that for the five half-years preceding August 31, 1891, the
bona fide sales of the Freehold Land Investment Company
amounted to only £30,057, while fancied sales—those
made with other companies of the group—were set down
at £415,850. The outgoings of the company for the same
period in excess of receipts were £158,567, and yet
£111,148 was paid in dividends, the directors taking no
less than £67,437, upon sales which proved to be fictitious
ones. The profit was assessed at £88,775, in addition to
which interest was credited to profit and loss. During
the same period £122,775 was charged as interest to
unsold properties and treated as realised assets.” In the
face of revelations like this, it begins to be doubtful if
there was any real boom at all. When a land co. could do
no genuine business even in the alleged boom period, and
only lived on paper by forming bogus companies, and
selling its properties to them at fabulous prices, and tak-
ing bills for the money—well knowing the bills wmuld
never be paid, and that the sales might as well have been
made to a corpse—it seems questionable if the darned
thing ever existed except in the offices of the Davies and
Munro “ banks.”
* * *
The city man of the Times recently made
comparisons between the banking systems of Australia
and England—the one relying upon the principle of
association in times of difficulty, the other depending
upon the assistance of the Bank of England, which in
turn looks to the Government Treasury in periods of crisis.
He quotes Bagehot in support of the Australian principle,
which he calls “ natural,”'but thinks the English system
is not wfithout its advantages.

It is an open secret in Melbourne that Shiels’

# * *
The wild cat mines of N.S.W. have had a big
weeding-out this year. Few mines but paying ones are
now working, simply because shareholders absolutely
refuse to pay calls, and as nine-tenths of the companies
are “no liability” there is no chance to enforce payment.
It is a hard time for mining managers, scores of whom are
unemployed, and many of them too poor to get away
from the country which they rushed in the boom. Up-
wards of a hundred, including some really good men,
recently applied for a £5-a-week berth.
# ft *
There is one Maoriland field which receives a
lot of Sydney newspaper puffing. We have read a hundred
times that the Try Fluke has returned ten times its paid-
up capital in dividends without any allusion to the
amount of its paid-up capital. It may be only sixpence a
share. The Great Mercury appears to be a payable mine,
but nothing to rave about. The reefs are numerous and
variable, the gold of low grade and so fine as to render
the saving of more than 70 per cent, economically difficult.
Great things are predicted of other two-men-and-a-ho.v
shows there, which appear to have only a hand-to-mouth
existence.
# # *
Already this year in N.S.W. over <£30,000 of
hard State cash has been spent in alleged prospecting.
And for what returns? Tell us of one good show un-
earthed by prospecting aid. *'30,000 would have gone far
towards establishing a big mining college, to open up a
new profession in N.S.W., where men could be well
trained and ore well-treated.

Bendigo has been having a good time. Divi
dends have exceeded calls, abandoned ground is beim

a falling tendency.
# * *
Dear Bulletin, —About 40 miles from Charters
Towers there is a silver-mine on the Star River, called the
Hero, owned by a Sydney Co., who have spent £20,000,
one way or the other, on the property and its develop-
ment. The manager himself, being fully convinced,
strove to convince the rest that the mine contained
enough silver to make a nation rich. He also got some
silver and made the shares go up, but the shareholders
got demoralised by his glowing reports and forgot to pay
the last call with that promptness, &c. And so the work
stopped right there, and the mine was hung up till further
orders. It is said the Co. has decided on dressing the ore
now above ground and all they can find below, though the
exact date is not mentioned. We, in Dead Man’s Gully,
are much excited over the report. Arniirim.
-jf*
The market value of all big mines swings like a
pendulum. Public madness, fomented in its greed by
those who could measure the mines, carried the Broken
Hill claims to a value they will never reach again. People
who bought in the very youth of the boom did so because
they believed “there was going to be a boom.” Ask the
scores of ruined Queenslanders why they bought Mount
Morgan shares on an aggregate value of seventeen
millions, and you are told : “ We were quite convinced
that they would see double the money.” They mathe-
matically measured the mine, and based the worth of the
millions of tons of rock which remained in the mountain
on the value of the tew thousands of tons taken out. No
allowance for depreciation is ever made in a boom. Yet
there never was a mine in the earth without a choice spot,
and never a mine that did not work that choice spot as
soon as discovered. How some men must have chuckled
and grown fat in “ selling as a favour ” Mount Morgans at
£l7, Broken Hills at £l4, Souths and Centrals at more
pounds than they now are shillings !

* # #
Lawyer Crick’s public narration of the <£2-10s.-
a-week-clerk-in-.a-chaff-shed who started a bogus bank in
Sydney and got deposits totalling £76,000 from the poor
public, is good reading. “ Whilst the money came in,
how he spent it! He made friends in every direction,
contested Parliament, and paid the expenses of another
candidate who, he thought, might drag him in, biit a dis-
cerning public left them both out.” The Parkes Govern-
ment made him a J.P. ; he is now in gaol. He ran such
a mucker that, when on trial, “ he hadn’t enough money
to pay a second-rate attorney for his defence.” That was
a crime.

which Simpson’s graphic report ought to have simplified.
# # #
No sooner was the Broken Hill strike settled
than the contractors on the British mine attempted to
abolish the 20 minutes “cut ” or spell for tucker in the
eight hours. Had they succeeded their next game would
likely have been to compel the men to put in a full eight
hours on the face, which meant, in most cases, a loss of an
hour or more in walking to and from the shaft.
* * #
The Northumberland (N.S.W.) miners seem
averse to the proposed alterations in the 1888 agreement
as embodied in that now submitted, fearing that every
amendment is an insidious attack on their rights as
unionists, and a covert, though none the less effectual,
attempt to entail a reduction in wages. The proprietors
feel the urgent necessity for stimulating trade by reducing
selling price, and consequently lowering wages, while the
man act on the principle ’Us belter to “ bear those ills we
have," &c., and therefore resist the excision or altera-
tion of any of the old clauses.
* * *
The farce of holding a school-like examination
at N.S.W. Mines Department for the appointment of
mining inspectors reminds the writer of the time when
the Victorian mining law compelled every mine engine-
driver to have a certificate, and to pas-f an examination
to get it. There were men who had been driving all their
lives who knew nothing of the technical requirements.
One who had been at the game 30 years appeared before
the examiners, some of whom had received their practical
training under him, nevertheless, they questioned him ;
“You have been driving many years ?” “Yes.” “Did
you ever have an accident?” “Yes.” “Many?”
“Yes.” “Did you ever have any through over-wind-
ing?” “Yes.” “ But did you ever have one serious, pre-
ventible accident? ” “Yes.” He passed.

It’s hard to respect grey hairs when they
get “into the soup.”
It is forty-six years since Sir Alfred
Stephen was knighted.
Robertson, a democrat, is expected to be
the next Mayor of Adelaide.
Like Sir James Martin, Sir Charles Lilley
was an attorney before being called to the Bar.
The heir-presumptive to the shaky throne
of Austria is expected to reach Sydney in March.
The bearded Archbishop Redwood is said
to be the wealthiest ecclesiastic in the Southern
Seas.
A hallelujah lass 7 feet high, the converted
Croagingolong giantess, sells War Orys in Mel-
bourne.
The staff of Melbourne Herald wants to
know when the M’Kinley tariff is to be abolished
in their office.
The Evergreen Butters has made a fourth
trip to the altar. This time with Miss A. For-
wood, late of Fiji.
The three chief Melbourne booksellers,
Mullen, Robertson, Cole, all arrived the same
day, just 40 years ago.
A Melbourne paper, referring to the death
of Sir James M‘Bain, speaks of “the lacerated
hearts of bereaved citizens.”
According to the Barrier Miner, Phil.
Lorimer, the bush poet and nomad, is brother to
the Victorian ex-Minister of Defence.

The three greatest decorative artists in
England—William Morris, Walter Crane, and
Henry Holiday—are all aggressive Socialists.
The father of Victorian attornies is Frank
Stephen (admitted 1840), solicitor to the Mel-
bourne Corporation, and nephew to Sir Alfred.
Ash, the brainy young (S.A.) independent
member, is nothing if not literally exact. He
once moved to amend a clause by the omission of
the letter t, and dotting three i’s.
Baron Hirsch is said to be the real buyer
of the Pall Mall Gazette—for <£50,000. Money
is no object to him—he first tried to buy the
Times, and then the Daily News.
George C. Hawker, M.P. for North
Adelaide, was gazetted a J.P. for South Australia
on Feb. 11, 1842. And yet he expects to win
election to Parliament again next year.
Zeal, the new President of the Vic. Legis-
lative Council, as a bachelor is to be commis-
erated. He will have positively none on whom
to cast the blame in accepting the inevitable
British title.
Tragedian Creswick lives in Melbourne, in
the person of an ex-barber; Disraeli, in a money-
lender ; Gladstone, in a Prahran Sunday-school
superintendent; Plon Plon Napoleon, in banker
F. G. Smith.
Robert Homburg, S. A. Attorney-General,
is now alleged to possess a dress-suit for the first
time in his life. He used to boast that he never
wore a bell-topper or a dress-suit, and never went
to Government House.
Magistrate-undertaker Raven, of Colling-
wood, Melb., is dead. It was he who buried
Johnny Fawkner. His Worship now buries him-
self ; the uninitiated wondered to read the funeral
notice without any undertaker.
Playford caused roars of laughter in S.A.
Assembly the other night, when remarking that
“the London banks never raised a finger to help
us ” —by elevating in emphasis his own digit, equal
in size to the average fat man’s thumb.
Now that Griffith has jumped the Q.
Chief Justiceship, the affronted senior puisne
Judge (Harding) will retire on becoming eligible
for his pension, twelve months hence. Bruin will
probably join the ranks of the English absentees.
A certain very active Melbourne J.P.,
who died last week, once said to a clerk of petty
sessions: “I hear a lot about common lor and
statoot lor. I’ve got the statoots at ’ome, but
where’s the book of common lor? I’d like to git
it.”
“In South Australia, the Bible Christians
have increased 50 per cent, in the last decade—
twice as fast as any other body.” See what one
solitary bachelor can do—provided he’s a Chief
■Justice with a beastly lot of private wealth besides
his salary!
Almost as soon as you receive this, writes
our London correspondent, the great Clement
Scott will be in Australia—on a health-voyage.
No doubt he will write a book, slating above all
the “ working man,” who is supposed to be driv-
ing Australia headlong to the devil.
R.C. Auxiliary Bishop O’Mahony, of
Toronto, Canada, is dead. He was the first
Bishop of Armidale (N.S.W.), but had some dis-
agreement—over a woman, ’twas said—with the
authorities; went to Eome, waited two years to
have his case heard at'the Vatican, was acquitted,
and appointed to Canada.
A correspondent : The late Thomas
Woolner, the sculptor, had, years ago, a studio
in Sydney which I often visited when a boy, with
my father. It was somewhere at the top end of
the Riley Estate, and the celebrated “round
house,” a sort of casino, was afterwards erected
on the same ground. Woolner could mould a
plaster bust of Wentworth with his eyes blindfold,
and make a good likeness too. This was a feat he
often performed.
In the race for the great prize of popularity
Wolfe’s Schnapps takes the lead.
Advice to Young Men —Dr. Richards’ ad., p. 21.
Labouchere on one occasion had to meet
at an English country station his valet, who was
to bring his master’s keys from Truth office.
The train arrived with the news that the unfor-
tunate valet had fallen from the train en route
and been cut to pieces. “Oh, indeed,” said
Labby, “do you think I could find the piece that
has my keys attached?”

kins, by holding to his sea-lordship till next Sept.,
secures a O.S. pension of .£5OO yearly in addition
to .£B5O as a naval pension. He is the joker who

A certain well - advertised Queensland
scientist whose name is in all men’s mouths in
bad weather, on a recent visit to the back-blocks,
proceeded to measure a gin in the interests of an-
thropological science. Ere he had completed his
task, the unsophisticated gin began to resent the
business, and, “ sooling” on her mates, imprisoned
the savant in the telegraph-office till the advent
of the line-repairer emboldened him to emerge.

appears in the list. One might guess why in the
case of Munro ; but what had Sir Edward Brad-
don done? asks The Bulletin correspondent.
Yet John Burns was invited. They are trying
hard to get at Democratic John, and hope in a
year or two to find him a staunch Tory.

Sir Theodore Martin may
be the coming Poet Laureate.
He translated Horace well, and
has written some beautiful adap-
tations from Heine. But his
really great work has been in
cobbling the books written by
Queen Victoria, for whom he has
long acted as literary dyer and
cleaner. Patches gracefully super-
imposed, and buttons sewn on
without extra charge.
Attorney - General Barton
was lately “ taken down ” by an
old bush trick. He was coming
down from Grafton with Governor
Jersey and a few political swells,
when a cheeky commercial came
aft to where the “silver-tails”
were seated, almost blubbering
oyer a splinter that had got into
his thumb through a stumble on
the fo’c’s’l. Taking out a pearl-
handled knife “ Toby ” proceeded
to remove the cause of the bag-
man’s woe. He got it out all
right, and Gov. Jersey nearly had
a fit when the Attorney-General
staggered backward to the bul-
warks, after hauling out with in-
finite pain one of the whisks,
at least a foot long, that had
recently formed part of an Ameri-
can bass-broom. Every “ fly ”
person knows the joke : you push
the point of the splinter under a
piece of loose skin and hide the
other end with your other hand
and coat-sleeve.

even than English people.”

The late Carl Feilberg, of Brisbane
Courier, was very fond of fishing. He was also
very near-sighted. One Saturday afternoon he
and a friend went down the river on a fishing trip,
and having caught five whiting by about tea-time,
went ashore to cook them. Feilberg fixed the fish
on the gridiron, and, turning to his friend, said :
“ Oh, well, there will be three apiece for us, any-
how.” “No,” replied the other ; “how can that
be —there are only five ?” “ Five ?” said Feilberg,
counting them—“one, two, three, four, five, six.
Six, sonny.” They had a oan-opener shaped
something like a fish, and poor Feilberg was cook-
ing it on the gridiron along with the other com-
ponent parts of the repast.
Campbell, editor and proprietor of
Western Champion (Q.) is among the wits of
Bananaland. Some years ago he dined in com-
pany with Dr. Blank, a then recent convivial and
garrulous addition to the Barcaldine medical staff.
The doctor improved the occasion of a pause
between the courses by explaining the mysterious
illness of a common friend. Dipping his index
finger in his wine-glass—it was half-full of cham-
pagne—he marked upon the table-cloth a diagram
intended to represent the patient’s internal
apparatus. “This,” said the draughtsman, trium-
phantly, “is an accurate description of our friend’s
condition.” “Yes, indeed,” replied Campbell,
“and illustrated by Phiz.”

James Cowie, last of the notable early
fathers of Victoria, died last week, aged 84, and
the Argus gave the old gentleman the half-column
obituary notice to which he had established his
claim more than forty years ago. James Cowie's
final appearance as an “ identity ” was at the
recent Melbourne Corporation jubilee dinner,
where he figured as the most interesting relic of
public life. He was Mayor of Geelong when
Governor La Trobe laid the foundation-stone of
the local railway terminus to the first line con-
structed in Victoria, a member (and for some
years sole survivor) of the original nominee
Council of the province, and again a member of
the first Legislative Assembly after Separation.
James Cowie had “made a rapid fortune in
Australia” before most of the “representative”
Victorians of to-day were born, certainly before
any of them were talked about. In 1848, he
issued a small paper currency which ranked as
equal to coin in his district. What inhabitant of
the Cabbage Garden could do as much to-day ?
Cowie dropped out of public notice when public
men began to be windbags, and a clean personal
character was no longer estimated at its proper
value, but he remained a shrewd, albeit rather
cantankerous old Radical up to the day of his
death. Many of his reminiscences have supplied
material for Bulletin paragraphs, and as an
authority on the ancient history of Victoria’s
“ first families,” James Cowie was invaluable.

The M.M. Co.’s splendid steamer, Poly-
nesien, beat the Orient Co.’s Ophir by nine hours,
in a race between Adelaide and Albany, W.A.,
making the record passage (60 hrs. 40 m.); and
the Union Co.’s s.s. Mararoa has just steamed
from Wellington, M.L., to the N.Z. wharf, Sydney,
in 81 hours, the fastest on record—an average
of 15.3 knots per hour for 1239 knots. She has
since beaten the splendid new steamer Warrimoc
in a 120-mile race on the Maoriland coast.
“OH DON’T TOUCH ME! DON’T COME
NEAR ME!”
These words were uttered with a howl—almost a
yell. Yet the boy to whom they were addressed
wasn’t within 10 feet of the howler, and wouldn’t
have come closer for his life. The scene was a big
business office in New York, and the howler was
the chief man in it. He owned the concern, and
was very rich, and a decent fellow enough. But
sometimes he would break out like that, and howl
as though he had just discovered a fire in a
powder-mill. You could hear him from the base-
ment to the roof. What was the matter with
him? Temporary insanity ? Not quite, but some-
thing nearly as bad. He had an acute attack of
gout in his toe, and at those solemn crises he
couldn’t bear the sight of even a shadow moving
in his direction. Ask somebody who has the gout
how it feels. Fancy a blacksmith twisting your
toe with hot pincers while a shoemaker is thrust-
ing a bradawl through your knee-joint. That’s a
little like it.
Well, there are things not so bad as gout, yet
they make us touchy enough. Here comes a man,
for instance, who says, “ Everything now was a
trouble to me.” What should he talk that way
for ? Why should everything have been a trouble
to him ? There is an old saying that while we
can’t keep the crows from flying, we needn’t let
them make nests in our hair. That’s good sense.
But it’s easy to give advice and to quote proverbs.
How does a person act who suffers from boils ?
Now, the foundation of all feeling and pain is
the nerves. An hour or two of toothache is a
lesson on the nervous system. But there are
diseases (or one disease anyhow) in which all the
nerves in the body seem to tingle to every sight
and sound. The mind is on the look-out for evil
—the man is depressed and afraid. Every word
means mischief, and every bush hides an enemy.
So he thinks. He knows what Solomon meant
when he said, “The grasshopper is a burden.”
Mr. Michael McCormack is a railway messenger
and lives m Mullingar, County Westmeath,
Ireland. In June, 1890, he was taken ill. His
mouth tasted foul and coppery, his stomach was
sour and dead, and when he forced down a little
food he felt so much distress and pain after it that
he was sorry he hadn’t let it alone and gone
hungry. Besides this, there were pains wander-
ing through his chest, back, and sides, hurting
him, biting here and there like ugly dogs loose in
a town. His head swam with dizziness, and he
couldn’t go to his work. All his ambition and
energy were gone out of him, and he would
scarcely have exerted himself even if he had been
suddenly promoted from the position of messenger
to that of station-master of the biggest station on
the railway.
“After a while,” he goes on to say, “a dull
heavy pain struck me in the back, so I couldn’t
stoop over. What I suffered from this and the
other things put together, I have no words to
describe. I had six months of it, and it was like
six years. In such a case a man takes medicines;
all he is told about. This I did, without getting
any good from them, and I got weaker and weaker.
Everything was a trouble to me; I couldn’t bear
things I used to think nothing of.
“In December, 1890, just before Christmas it
was, I first heard of Mother Seigel’s Syrup and
what it had done in cases like mine. I got a
bottle from Mr. Rogers’ Drug Stores, and before
I had used all of it I felt wonderfully better ; and
by keeping on with it a short time every pain and
ache went out of me, and I was able to go about
my work as well as ever I was in my life.” These
facts are vouched for by H. Rogers, Esq., Town
Commissioner, Mullingar.
Now, what made messenger McCormack’s
nerves so sensitive, and his life so miserable for
six months? Indigestion and dyspepsia; the
same detestable malady that does the same ill
turn for millions of others, men and women, of all
sorts and conditions. Plenty of them will read
this true and simple story, and our opinion is—
founded on the best of proofs—that if they try the
remedy which cured McCormack they w'ill come
out of it as happily as he did. But the sooner the
better.

Dr. Von Miguel, Prussian Minister of
Finance, has in hand a scheme for the gradual
nationalisation of ground-rents and of the un-
earned inorease in land-values. The Commissioners
are to be allowed to tax these two forms of wealth
according to their necessities. Should his scheme
be adopted it will be the longest stride yet taken
in the direction of practical socialism.

Sydney Echo started out the other day to
demonstrate that the famous M’Kinley tariff had
ruined the tinplate industry in the United States.
Since then the official returns from the U.S.
Treasury demonstrate that the American produc-
tion of tinplate has risen from 800,000 lbs for the
quarter ending 30th September, ’9l, to 8,000,000
lbs. for the quarter ending 30th June, ’92.
Grainger, M.L.A. (S.A.) has carried a
motion, against the strong opposition of the
Downer Cabinet, that a legacy duty of 10 per
cent, should be levied on all legacies of .£20,000
and upwards, and that a similar duty should be
levied when the estate is conveyed by deed of gift
instead of by will. The latter is the favourite
system whereby moribund rich men dodge the
legacy-tax.
The N.S.W. Railway Commissioners have
sent round a sort of round-robin, in which the
employes are requested to express their willingness
to give up their “home passes’’after 31st Dec.,
and pay half-fare, in future, when they travel
homewards at the end of a day’s work. No man
would willingly sign such a document, and it
simply means that the railway-servant who refuses
to sign must find work elsewhere.
J. H. Gordon, M.L.0., perhaps the most
eloquent and certainly not the least clear-headed
man in S.A. politics, has assigned his estate, and
resigned his seat in the Council—debts, £IB,OOO,
assets, £12,000. Though a solicitor he has the
confidence of the Trades and Labour Council, and
will probably go to the Assembly as a city member
next March. A Labour candidate will probably
run for his late seat.

broth ef-Turner of Premier Dibbs, would, it is
cabled, settle Australian labour disputes by ex-
porting to Australia “ fresh labour under a
properly organised scheme of State emigration.

Alfred Deakin, in the Victorian Assembly,
quoted a “ free gift preamble,” or some such orna-
ment of our blessed constitution, on a recent night.
It is improving reading and very characteristic of
the monarohial system—" We, your Majesty’s
most dutiful and loyal subjects, the Legislative-
Assembly, towards making good the supply which
we have cheerfully granted to your Majesty, have
resolved to grant unto your Majesty the sum here-
inafter mentioned, and do therefore most humbly
beseech your Majesty it may be enacted.”
Of the 54 members in the S.A. Assembly,
it is expected that at the general election next
March these will be rejected Sir E. T. Smith,
Messrs. Bartlett, Blacker, Caldwell, Oatt, Cock,
Henry Downer, Giles, Gould, Grayson, Hack,
Hancock, Handyside, Hawker, Hooper, Hopkins,
Johnson, J. R. Kelly, Lake, Lamshed, Landseer,
M'Donald, Miller, Osman, Parsons, Playford
(unless he again changes his district), Rounsevell,
Stock, and White. Of the other half, the doubt-
ful men are; Messrs. Brooker, Butler, Horn,
Moule, and Soherk, the seats of the remainder
being considered certain.

Says Sydney Echo, grabbing at the accus-
tomed straw : “The universality of smuggling
among females is a testimony to their hearty
sympathy with Freetrade,” and then the dismal
publication points to this as a solid evidence that
Protection is wrong, and should be abolished.
And seemingly it never dawns on the editorial
horizon that the universality of stealing and lying
equally proves that the laws against these things-
should be abolished, and that, on the same prin-
ciple, when aby crime, from garrotting and in-
fanticide down to heaving bricks at a blackleg,
becomes sufficiently common it ought to be
legalised right off.
The N.S.W. Parliamentary rule never to
take any new business after 11 p.m. was always use-
less. It gave the stonewallers every show to block
the transaction of work. In Victoria, Gaunsonand
Bent (now Speaker) often played with the House
for nights together, with one eye apiece on the
clock. At the stroke of 11, they resumed their
seats and were satisfied. The idea of this 11
o’clock rule was that a Government should not
spring a surprise on a thin House at a late hour.
Far better that than the present inertia. Legis-
lators are paid to be present, and, since they won’t
agree to work during the daytime, they are
expected to be in their places, no matter how long
the House chooses to sit. The present N.S.W. rule
is framed in the interests of a set of men whose
private business takes precedence of public affairs.
The Schey-condemnation resolution was
passed in the N.S.W. Assembly after 4 a.m.
Before that hour the first edition of Sydney Daily
Telegraph was in the hands of hon. members,
with a “leader” commencing
In placing on record last night its severest condemna-
tion of the conduct of the hon. member for Iledfern, Mr.
Schey, &c.
Now, suppose the division had fluked the other
way? The leader-writer has marvellous luck ; a
common reporter’s prediction would have been
falsified. The writer once undertook to report a
Kew concert for a Melbourne paper, “ from the
programme,” only to see next morning in a rival
daily—“ Owing to the inclemency of the weather,
the Kew concert was postponed.” This, after he
had judiciously marmaladed every notable per-
former, was too much joy, and he thankfully
resigned his billet without entering into explana-
tions.
Why is it that death should come before
the allotted age of “three score and ten,” and
why so many millions of human beings never live
to three score and five, and why that not one in
every thousand live to three score years? We
kill ourselves. Many intei'esting particulars are
found in tabh s of physiologists, and writers
on hygiene. The eminent physiologists, Haller
and Buffon, present interesting particulars on the
subject of longevity. They treat it in two ways,
historical and physical. The historical side of the
recitation of all facts known of the naturally
ordinary and extreme duration of life, and the
physical aspect of the problem involves the con-
templation of the great natural phases of develop-
ment of the species, digestion, period of growth,
and length of life. Mankind pays too little
attention to the warning voice of nature; signs of
approaching calamity are unheeded; the dis-
organisation of the affected organ increases and
after a time becomes insensible ; then no pain is
felt, and the sufferer is deluded into the belief
that be has shaken off the disease. Nothing
could be more erroneous or dangerous, for the
complaint has only passed on one further stage
towards the end, and nature ceases to send those
Warning aches and pains as they were unnoticed
when sent; the debility and weakness increases
however, and nervousness, loss of appetite, and
general languor comes on, and a cold is caught in
the kidneys, lungs, or liver, and another life is
sacrificed to Bright’s disease, consumption, or
some other serious and fatal disease. Scoffers can
talk as they like at the virtues claimed for
Clements Tonic, but even the most prejudiced
men know it is genuine and does possess merits no
other medicine ever did, an instance in the case of
Mrs. J. Bruce, 4 Victorian Terrace, Prymont
Bridge-road, Glebe, New South Wales, who
writes:—For the last 40 years I have suffered
excruciating agony with toothache, bilious head-
ache, indigestion, &c. Although having a sound
set of teeth, I have had toothache for a fortnight
at a time. I could not touch solid food ; the only
nourishment I could get was through taking beef-
tea, &c., and sucking it through a straw. I could
not get up till late in the day, and then it was
only for a short time, and I had to lie down again.
As for trying to do my domestic duties, it was
simply an impossibility ; it even seemed too great
a trouble for me to dress myself, so languid and
ill I felt. A short time ago I was persuaded to
take Clements Tonic. The effect was miraculous;
the toothache, headache, and tired feeling dis-
appeared after taking a couple of bottles. Now I
do all my house-work, although over 60 years old,
the. work seems a pleasure. I advise everyone
that suffers as I did to take it, as it certainly
cured me, and I cannot thank the proprietor
enough for the benefit I derived from it.—l am,
yours gratefully, Mrs. J. Bruce, 4 Victoria
Terrace. Pyrmont Bridge-road, Glebe, New South
Wales,

Society
Tattersall’s biggest prize, £27,000, fell this
year to “ Jack” Layton, one of the leaders in the
great shearers’ strike of 1890, who up to the early
part of this month was far away in the North
West of Queensland, over 1200 miles from Bris-
bane. When the good news reached him he wasopal-
digging in the Adavale Ranges, and he dropped
his pick to scan the result-list. His great luck
didn’t upset him—“though, of course, I felt a
little excited for two or three minutes.” He is a
bachelor—a bashful, brawny, six-foot Scotchman,
30 years of age, and sober as a judge—a good bush-
man, a “ gun ’’shearer, aminer, fencer, horse-breaker
—everything. He has been in Queensland some
nine years, and has spent nearly the whole time in
the Adavale district. He leaves Sydney for
Scotland on 28th instant; three mates have come
with him to see him off. The news of his good
fortune preceded him all the way from Adavale
where his fellow-workers turned out to giv§ him a
send-off, to Sydney, where he has banked his pile.
He intends, after visiting Scotland, to return to
Queensland —where he will have no objection to
stand as a labour-member. Of Northern labour-
troubles he speaks with much feeling.
* * *
A well-known man was taken suddenly ill
in the Adelaide Speaker’s gallery and carried
insensible out of the House. The doctors sum-
moned agreed that the seizure was attributable to
excessive tea-drinking. He used to take about
eight cups of Chinese tea per diem. Since
“ swearing off ” he has gained 141bs. in weight,
and has never felt better in his life.

Auckland (M,L.) Court, when the Tenny-
son Cole trouble was on, drew a kindly distinction
between the two ladies involved. The legitimate
one had been known as “Mrs.” (English and
virtuous) Cole, the other one as “Madame”
(French and wicked) Cole, and the Court took
them at that. “ Madame ”is good. But if there
had been three of them what would they have
called the other one?

Dear Bulletin, — It is psychologically and
pathologically curious to note that the Sydney
girl, Maidment, who declares that “an angel,”
like unto her dead mistress, showed her where a
certain will was secreted, prefaces her statement
with—“ I teas not very well on Monday, and was
lying down on the sofa,’ &c., &e. In all alleged
supernaturalism, the clairvoyant, or vision-seer,
has generally to be in bad health before the
mysteries phenomenalise themselves. Thus, your
dipsomaniac sees no visions at all until ho ceases
his bout, and begins to “suffer a recovery.” The
writer has known such a one who will tell you
that he knows what he sees are illusions, but he
will dictate to you word for word what his (to
others invisible) dramatis persona are saying, and
will describe their actions. At other times he will
resolve into a cataleptic state for periods varying
from 20 minutes to four or five hours. Waking,
he will make certain announcements concerning
certain people he has visited —involuntarily, for he
has no choice Of selection —hundreds of miles
away, during his catalepsy. So far, these
announcements have always been Verified by afteri
neWs, and the events haVe happened as he
described. A curioUs feature is that if he does
not tell the nature of his psychic discoveries im-
mediately on waking, and while Still in a semi-
confused state, he forgets them absolutely as
thougl) they had never been. These are facts
within my personal knowledge. Can the medical
faculty explain them ? Must one get ill before one
is a clairvoyant ?— Dipso.

SEASONABLE ITEM.
HIGGINGS : “Hallo, Dobbs, you’re looking seedy; are you feeling
unwell?”
DOBBS (inst back from races , dejectedly): “ No, I’m feeling the bottom
of my pocket.”
Jealous Melbourne once more ! Sydney
produces its girl-slayer and suicide, bagnio murder,
a crop of heart-punctured babies, and several other
horrors, when, pouf! in walks Melbourne with a
three-volumed romance, in which the villain (who
fails to kill his woman and her husband, but suc-
cessfully suicides) uses, with picturesque effect, an
axe, with which he smashes his way into his
paramour’s house, an iron bar with which lie
breaks furniture into flinders, two revolvers, a
dagger and a phial of prussic acid. He climbs
into an engine-house and blazes away generally
like a stage hero, and then dies suddenly of prussic
acid as the police rush to overpower him. Sydney
throws up the sponge.
Did you notice how easily the intended female
victim of Melbourne’s latest hero eluded him ?
“Put out the candle, Ike.” He put it out, and
she slipped easily into the darkness. The man-
victim just stood his ground till he felt a bullet
graze him, and then bolted. One cool-headed
woman’s stratagem any time is worth the ordinary
pluck of a dozen average men, who will stand their
ground till they feel the sting of the lead, and
then—well, it depends on liver, conscience, beer
and lots of things women don’t use.
The passengers on a very swell Anglo-
Australian liner were at a loss to find out how it
was they could hear whispering in the cabin of a
French governess, Who was supposed to occupy it
all by her own little self. Sometimes they would
take it in turns to watch all night; and in the
morning, would have the pleasure of seeing the
little governess open the door, smile, and trip
gaily along the deck, leaving the door wide open
fot them to ascertain that no contraband male
was stowfid inside. One night, when they plainly
heard a man’s voice, the key of the cabin was ort
the outside. They turned it, and virtuously sat
down to wait the result. In the morning they all
assembled at the cabin-door to see the brazen hussy
found out, when a voice inside asked fot someone
to unlock the door. A fat old frump, With two
skinny daughters, set her mouth severely, stepped
forward, End turned the key. The little governess
tripped but, thanked het sweetly, smiled on
the expectant crowd, and walked off, leaving them
to search the cabin. It was empty. Yet every
night during the voyage they could hear a man’s
voice in the cabin ; but no one ever found out who
it was, or how he got there. P.S. —The ship had
pbrtholes.

Dear Bulletin, —Not long ago we could
send letters through the Victorian post for a
penny, and to N.S. W. for 2d. an ounce. Now it
costs us 4d. to get an ounce letter to Sydney, and
our minimum local postage rate is 2d. I think I
can see a time coming when the cheapest way of
corresponding with a friend who lives in an
adjoining suburb will be via Hingland. Already
it is the surest way. “ Mail ” letters seem to be
held rather sacred, whilst inter-suburban letters
are mostly prigged by the postman who “ clears ”
them at the pillar-box, or burnt, for the sake of
saving time, by the postman who undertakes to
deliver them.—Yours, Melbourne.

The Mayor of Adelaide (Bullock) has
greatly offended many members of the Legislative
Assembly by leaving them out of his invitation
lists. This nearly caused the House to sit on the
night of his official ball, while it definitely declined
to adjourn for the sake of his banquet.

A lady urges in a Melb. religious paper
that Bibles should be placed in all the Coffee
Palace bedrooms, and, after that, in all the hotels.
Quite right, and the proper order of procedure.
Judge Windeyer (N.S. W.) —“A man
could not be too circumspect when visiting a
woman who was living apart from her husband.
However innocent the parties might be, it was a
most imprudent thing to do.” Kind gentlemen
anxious to relieve grass widows in distress should
paste this in their hats. They should also paste
in their hats this further maxim : If you would
keep out of trouble, never visit a woman of any
kind, at any time.
* * »
Bishop Camidge (Bathurst, N.S.W.) is an
artist in casuistical distinctions. He disapproves
of bazaars to raise stipends or build churches, but
has no objection to bazaars for church improve-
ment ; he thinks, however, the promoters should
be content with a profit of not more than 200 or
300 per cent. From which you would almost think
his reverence was running not a church but a
synagogue.

There was a surprise-party the other night
at the Waverley (Sydney) home of Mr. J. I.
Hunt, a well-known pressman. The artists noise-
lessly “dropped in” and plagiarised about <£ls
worth of plate, books, &c. But, after all, the
proceedings of this surprise-party, the victim
cheerfully admits, contrast favourably with those
of the social surprise-party. They came without
ostentation, above all without noise, eulogy, or the
eleemosynary haul so often dumped upon the
piand; and they departed without reciting
Beautiful Snow ” or “ Eugene Aram’s Dream;”

The S.M. Herald proprietary wish us to
contradict the statement in last week’s Bulletin
that the will of the late John Fairfax contained a
clause making the holding of the annual wayze-
goose mandatory. In a Word, the present pro-
prietors give the entertainment for no other reason
than that it pleases them to do SO; We haVb jUst
been reading this year’s wayzegoose report
in the HeeAld aforesaid, and see that
the toast of “The Firm of John Fairfax
and Sons ” was “received with a special degree of
enthusiasm,” and was “drunk with great enthu-
siasm j” that Mr. J. R. Fairfax was “ loudly
cheered that Mr. C. B. Fairfax was “well
received;” that Mr. Geoffrey Fairfax was “re-
ceived with very great warmth ■” that Mr. J. O.
Fairfax was “received with applause;” and that
the absent Mr. Edward Fairfax’s health was
“drunk with much cordiality.” Who wouldn’t
give a big spread under such inspiriting con-
ditions?
AN UNBELIEVER’S DIRGE.
With growth of years one loses faith
In things you hear and see :
The bogey and the graveyard wraith
No longer frighten me.
I don’t believe in “less than cost,”
Or “ bankrupt stocks," ye ken,
And all my earthly faith I’ve lost
In “much-respected” men.
I don’t believe in Noah’s Flood,
Or Jonah and thfe Whale ;
I’m not impressed by Royal blood.
Or thrilled by fairy tale.
I’m grieved to say the poet scoffs
At Dan in Lion’s dea,
And also at those starchy toffs,
The “ milch-respected ” men.
I don’t believe in balance-sheets,
Or Addie Conyers’ leg ;
I see poor beggars in the streets,
But not in them who beg.
Young women’s smiles convince me not
(Except just now and then),
And O ! how very, 11 full ” I’ve got
Of “ much-respected” men !
I don’t believe in priest or monk,
Nor in Tom Walker's trade ;
_ Scant joy I find in getting drunk
And less in lemonade.
I don’t believe in numbers odd,
Or even numbers ; but
I try to put my trust in Ood,
And haven’t much to put.

• * •
A Parramatta clergyman has just declared
that many church people would not live in some
houses erected for Divine worship; but they
thought them good enough for God. That’s so.
The Eternal is presumed to be free from liability
to rheumatism, sciatica, and lumbago.

• « «
Toowoomba (Q.) local optionists, led by
the local parsons, began the periodical crusade
against Drink last week. A poll was taken as to
closing the worst half-dozen of the 39 local pubs.
However, despite ecclesiastical opposition, the
publicans won the day by 17 votes. They had
taken the precaution to hire all the available cabs
and give their supporters the rare treat of a free
ride. The additional luxury of a free drink
decided many wavering voters.
* * m
In giving evidence with respect to the
circumstances attendant on the violent death of
the woman Nolan in Howard-street, Sydney, a
female witness touched upon one of those subtle
distinctions which have exercised the minds of
generations. Asked whether one of the persons
in the case was drunk at a certain time, she
replied that he was “not so drunk.” This quali-
fication is exactly in the spirit of the author of
“ Auld Lang Syne ” :
We are no fou, we’re no that fou.
It was the same witness who drew the fine dis-
tinction between being knocked insensible and
toeing knocked “ slightly ” insensible. It may be
difficult for an intelligence of less refinement to
perceive any difference between a person being
slightly insensible and slightly sensible.
• * •
Dear Bulletin,—Andy Kelly, M.P., is
mighty solicitous about the hours of the poor
prison warders who have to watch a condemned
man. The divil take Count O’Kelly. Think of the
hours of the prisoner ! In my opinion, the man
who would take any of these billets for hire—
soldier, bailiff, process-server, debt-collector, pro-
fessional copman, turnkey, flagellator, executioner
—deserves no sympathy for even deathly hours.
It may be exhilarating to be a man-hunter; but
to be a man-hunter at so much per day ! Faugh !
And then to expect to be treated like a man!
I’d cheerfully hang the lot, but not for pay.—
Yours, The Dipsomaniac.
The story goes that a man, who was for
over two months a washer-up in the kitchen of a
Sydney hospital, was three months ago taken to
the Coast hospital as an incurable leper. By the
way, a well-known doctor told us the other day
that he had often seen lepers among Lascar crews.

Lithographic prayers seem to have as little efficacy
as the oral variety.

If someone would introduce a bill provid-
ing that the eighteenpenny oranges in the shop
should be at least as large as the sixpenny ones in
the window, failing which the Italian who runs
the. place to be gaoled for three months, another
capacious public want would be supplied.
* . * #
The late Robert Dixon, of Melbourne,
whose will is being contested on the ground of bis
alleged insanity, first made the acquaintance of
Mrs. D. in connection with Wesleyan Sunday-
school work. Poor Robert remained Wesleyan
always, whereas his wife became converted and
learned to love dancing, the result being matri-
monial Sheol for 25 years. There’s a very evident
moral to these Dixon disclosures. Choose a girl
who has had her share of fun before marriage, in
preference to the creed-bound virgin who will
begin to dance directly she gets rid of the Sunday
school. Also, don’t attempt to cure your wife of
going out to the dance by stopping at home your-
self and getting drunk. Go out likewise and
waltz with all the giddiest little things you can
get your arm round. Then the chances are ten to
one that the rebellious lady will learn to hate
ballrooms, and soon lead you back by the ear to
your own fireside.
