“Poor Abigail!” says the parson-edited
Brisbane Telegraph; “that he alone should
suffer where so many have sinned.” Also, to put
the same idea another way : “Poor Deeming, that
he alone should be hanged where so many mur-
derers have escaped.” The logic of the parson-
ridden paper is an awful thing.
No wise patent-
medicine man ever
goes to law. When
he does, no matter on
what pretext, some
lawyer always rises
and asks him, on his
oath, if his nostrum
isn’t really composed
of aloes and gamboge,
or something like
that, and it takes six
months’ hard adver-
tising to do away
with the evil impres-
sion that results. In
Auckland (M.L.),
the other day, the
local agents for an
eminently successful
firm of British pill-
makers—one of those
firms whose pills are
a boon to the young
man who doesn’t feel
well, and the only
ones which can re-
move the disease that
comes stealing upon
usand produces symp-
toms akin to the above
—went into court
over a question of
Customs duty. Then
the solicitor on the
other side proved that
the gaudy medicine
was only made of
aloes and senna with
some flavouring
material thrown in,
and that anybody
could buy a barrel-
full of the precious
remedy at the nearest
chemists for about
4s. per lb., whereas
the patentees sold it
for something like
455., and the pill firm
seemed to be sorry it
spoke.
In a Southern Court, the other day,
a barrister begged that the hearing of a will-case
might be adjourned until next day, in order to
give him an opportunity of wading through a pile
of documents before delivering his opening speech.
His Honor, after some demur, consented to the
adjournment. “ I hope you will not think we are
wasting a day,” said the learned counsel, and his
Honor replied that he was sure the learned bar-
rister wouldn’t dream of wasting a day. He was
right—judges are never wrong. Two or three
hours later that barrister was seen on the race-
course backing a supposed “dead-bird” (which
didn’t come off) for the Handicap Trot. The
question seems to arise just here -is a case ever
postponed, at cost of money, time, and wear and
tear of anxiety to the poor devils of litigants, in
order that counsel may attend a pigeon-match ?

A man advertises in a London paper for a
good-looking female “to assist in starting a new
religion which is bound to be popular.” The old
battered, war-horse sort of woman who used to
help at starting new religions is out of date now.
It is the Gaiety girl variety that is needed to make
a creed boom these times.

at you because of your bad conduct.”

A picture of the “New Australia” region,
from the New York Mercury :
The sun, drooping to the horizon, threw his fiery rays
upon a burning sky and an almost boundless plain. The
parched earth was covered with a yellow dust. This
monotonous and melancholy pampa extends from the
confines of Patagonia to the suburbs of the city of Buenos
Ayres, spreading across many hundred leagues. There
was no spring, no verdure, no oasis in the desert. Not a
tree save the ombu, which is found in solitary state at
long intervals, and which is useless for either construction
or fuel; knotty and twisted, it resists the axe, and after
it is broken extinguishes the fire already kindled. When
the rainy season gives place to Summer, the ardent rays
of a sun never hidden or softened by the slightest clpud
burn to dust the hardy young leaves which have just
put forth. Not a drop of water in this dreary desert
except brackish or stagnant ponds, which were made by
the rains of winter, and which evaporate slowly in
infected odours. As far as the eye can see, the naked
plain touches the glassy horizon, sometimes deceiving the
traveller with the mirage so familiar to the Arabs of the
African Desert.
We begin to believe in the big land-grant now*
Almost any Government Would give away a big
slab of land like desert that is frozen in
winter, and burnt to ashes in summer, and full of
haggard melancholy all the year round.

A young Southern society lady, last year*
caused whispers among the denizens of a fashion-
able suburb by openly meeting a prominent foot-
baller whose station in life was hardly up to their
standard. The lowly lover has since been
appointed groom in the “old man’s ” household.
• • •
Mrs. Michel, the petitioner in that famous
Sydney divorce-case, is very seriously ill. It is
not long since her father, that big-hearted, broad-
minded man, Ebenezer Skinner, died. W. W.
Collins, the lecturer, is her brother-in-law.
* * *
The Austral Salon testimonialised Oliver
Wendell Holmes, and got an unctuous letter in
reply. The purpose and ambition of the Salon is
to send gaudy addresses to celebrities whenever
opportunity offers, and to waylay important
globe-trotters and “receive” them by force of
arms. By persisting in this line, it has grown to
believe itself quite an important concern.
TREASURE TROVE.
1st. ROBBER (who has been exploring another part of the house): “Well,
Bill, what yer found? I aint got nothing!”
2nd. ROBBER: “Well, I haint got nothing, either, ’cept this ’ere blooming
love letter. It orter stand us in a bit if ever the old gal goes for a
divorce.”
Ellery s (Vic.) forecast for Nov. Ist (Cup
day): “ Victoria —generally dull, cairn, with some
clouds, light south-west wind.” Wragge’s ditto:
“Victoria—overcast, heavy clouds, with heavy
showers and rain imminent near coast, wind east.”
Punters, to their sorrow, know now which was
correct.
* # *
Last week a wan female was arrested at
Newcastle for haunting the premises of the A. J.S,
Bank. She used to meander in at all hours,
and hysterically demand an interview with an
official, who, she stated, had promised to marry
her. Medical examination failed to discover any
trace of insanity, and she was accordingly dis-
charged. She was formerly employed as a domes-
tic at the bank.

The officers returned the fire, and for some time the

Stead now wants an Act of Parliament
compelling all parents cursed with boys only to
adopt at least one girl, and vice versa. In that
case, could one of the boys marry this adopted
sister, according to Stead ? It hardly- matters •
probably he’d know her so well he wouldn’t want
to. But what of those married people who have
no children?. Would they be expected to adopt
both a boy and a girl ? If so, this would make
marriage more hideous than ever.

forbids reference to any offender as a Jew.
THE ROYAL BABY.
Away across the billowy brine, in distant Germanie,
A mighty monarch holdeth sway beside the Baltic Sea;
No common mortal William is—nor made of common clay;
He is the pet of Providence—whate’er the scoffers gay.
He never wets his whistle, as do vulgarians vile,
But quaffs celestial nectar in a proud, Olympian style ;
He never eats his victuals, as we common earthworms do;
He gets ambrosia from the gods—and wit and wisdom,
too.
It chanced, of late, a baby came to cheer the monarch’s
life ;
We thought that William woufd proud, and so would
William’s wife ;
But, how we stood astounded when we heard his message
read—
“ The Lord has given me a girl,” the modest monarch
said.
No credit to the pater, and no credit to mamma,
For royal babies are not born as common babies are;
(You look surprised ? Well—yes—we’ve heard of wisdom
from on high,
But—bless us all!—whoever heard of babies from the sky?)
Away ye vulgar infants of the fat and fiery face !
Away ye bald anomalies—ye badges of disgrace!
Be silent, all ye pug-nosed pets 1 for now the poet sings
Of babies bright from realms of light, with haloes and with
wings.
Away—ye puling parents, with your darling in your
arms !
Away with all your doting talk about its little charms 1
Go—gaze on William’s baby in its little royal bed,
Then drop your infant down the sink—and hide your
guilty head.
P. LUFTIG

A Brisbane exchange, referring to the
Yankee Dr. Pierson’s rivalry for the London
Tabernacle pulpit with poor little Tommy
Spurgeon, the Auckland (M.L.) parson, points out
that it was a little too much to expect that a
people nursed in the belief that infant baptism
was superstitious would calmly accept as their
spiritual leader “ a child-sprinkling Presby-
terian.”
Frenchy, a well-known aborigine north of
Adelaide, thus gained his sobriquet: For, five
shillings given by an incredulous group of bag-
men, he took a run and smashed a hole through a
wooden door with his head. When the applause
had subsided, and Frenchy had drunk his ale, he
eloquently remarked : “Me show you plurry good
ting. You give him me two butcher beer, me
drive him nail in blanky board alonga my head-
front ” —and he tapped his head knowingly.
They agreed, and having procured a “F ren ch ”
nail, four inches long, he pegged away with his
forehead until it was flush with the bar-counter.
They gave him best.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
At the recent Q.T.C. meeting, a horse called
Orator paid the dividend of €159 ss. for a sovereign.
He had previously run unplaced in a race at a pony meet-
-111'-'. Queensland orators are equally deceiving, whether
they run on four legs or two.
# # *
In a cricket-match near Newcastle, N.S.W.,
last week, the middle stump Was knocked out of the
ground without disturbing lie bails. “Not out,” said
the umpire. Inspection showed that the bails (a new set)
were glued together, and had been placed on the wicket
in that state.
# # *
A. H. Gee, “Australian Foil,” of Melbourne,
backed Glenloth for Caulfield Cup, and told a friend whom
he laid on to it that at any rate they’d bo:.h back him for
the Melbourne Cup. Gee forgot, and the friend made a
big haul. Unsafe to talk Cup to Gee now.
* * #
John Corlett is responsible for the statement
that Joe Thompson lost £6O 000 over last year's Cam-
bridgeshire, trying to show English motallicians how to
do it Joe is very quiet now in London. ■
# # *
Chaffey Baker, C.M.G., the Atlas of the S.A.
National Ass, only had confidence enough in his dream of
Glenloth to win £4O on him.
# # *
Jonathan Brown, so long the superintendent of
garden arrangements at Flemington course, is dead and
buried. Glenloth’s awful Cup Day administered the last
shook to poor Jonathan.
# # *
Portsea, of course, was. certain to have w.on the
Melbourne Cup— if he hadn’t fallen. The horse, just like
G’Naroo last year, was passing the others with the
greatest ease. For a real racing certainty (bar accidents)
commend us to the “ dead bird ” that tumbles down.
* # *
Years ago in England a punter couldn’t go far
wrong at backing Stockweil’s “ get ” in all big weight-for-
age races. Trenton seems to be popping up into the
dtookwell of Australia. What’s the fancy price about
Trenton being the sire of the next A J.C. or V.R.C. Derby
winner ?
# # *
A EJemington-flat bookmaker got three months
with hard labour for eluding the pursuit of a woman who
took 20 half-sovereigns to 1 about Glenloth for the Cup.
A man who couldn’t do the square thing by Glenloth
must have been the welshiest of welshers, and no mistake;
But if the law is going to put down betting, why should it
not put in gaol that woman as well as the rascal she
gambled with? Why should the law give any measure of
protection at all to the taker of the odds?
* * *
Playing at Norton’s Summit (S, A.), Jack Lyotls
the other day made 101 runs in 38 minutes for the Nor-
woods. His score included 6 fives and 13 fours. This
must be about tile record.
# * •
Sir Edwin Smith, M.L.A., the wealthy Ade-
I ude brewer, is moving heaven and earth to induce George
Giffen to finally promise to join the next Australian
Eleven. Sir E Twin says if George promises to go, he will
go himself. Gillen promises to “seriously consider the
matter.”
* * *
South Australia will be strong in this year’s
interprovincial cricket contests. Besides the Giffens,
Lyons, Blimnan, and the other cracks, who are in great
forth, there are soiri? splendid younger players; Fred.
Jarvis, the Wicketkeeper’s brother, is batting better than
anyone in Adelaide just now. Reidman has made over
200 nliis in three innings, and Martin (a junior, who has
been watched for some time) in ide 90 last week. Those
Who have watched George Giffen at practice say he is
hatting better this year than ever in his life. So far he

Splendide, the four-year-old mare, which won
the Queensland Cup last week under the thumping weight
of9st. 21b., is little more than a pony. She was bought
as a yearling for 30 guineas, and has since won over £3OOO
in stakes alone.

FUNNY IRISH BLUNDERS.
The native of the Green Isle who said, “It’s
a great comfort to be left alone when your
sweetheart is with you,” and the Irish auctioneer
who, while praising some goods he was offering
for sale, exclaimed, “These are just the things I
should like to buy for my wife if she were a
widow,” made very witty bulls. Equally good,
but even more complicated, was that perpetrated
by the exile in America, who pathetically ex-
pressed himself thus : “Sure an’ if I live till I die,
an’ God knows whether I will or not, I’ll visit
ould Oireland afore I lave Philadelfy.” A funnier
blunder still, was that made by a new-chum
Hibernian, who was a passenger by the Southern
Express the other night. He alighted, with other
thirsty souls, at Mittagong, but lingered at the
bar, over his favourite nectar, longer than lie
should, and only regained the platform in time to
see the tail-lights of the train by which he intended
to have journeyed, receding from his view. For
a moment he was dumb with astonishment, and
then collecting all the energy and presence of
mind he was capable of exerting, he cried, “Hould
on there, ye blatherin’ engine ; faix, there’s a man
on board oi yo that’s left behind ! ” These
stories have all the true Irish ring about
them, and call up memories of the Irishman
who complained to his physician that he
“ gave him so many drugs he was sick a long
time after he got well.” No one who has used
St. Jacobs Oil can make a similar complaint to
that; it never causes a man to “feel sick after he
is well.” St. Jacobs Oil is an external remedy
for the absolute cure of pain, and it leaves no ill
effects behind. After using it the rheumatic
patient leaves his bed, or throws away his crutches
and begins life again a new man. Hear what Mr.
F. W. Kirby, of Mort-street, Balmain, says about
it; — “I use St. Jacobs Oil for rheumatism, and
do not know what I should do without it. The
kind of Work I aiU engaged in compels me to be
out in all weathers, and I often have to remaiii in
wet clothes for hours together. The result is that
I get rheumatism badly in my back at times. I
find there is nothing eqtial to St. Jacobs Gil fol*
shifting it, and putting me all right again. Both
my wife and I look upon it as a real blessing.”
The Voluntary testimony of thousands of such
witnesses proves that this marvellous remedy
possesses the confidence of all classes of the
community.

The Thief.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Among the wild fowl of Maoriland, the Maori-hen
and the pukaki are distinguished by their love for
glittering, objects. Like the ostrich, these birds
swallow such things as an aid to digestion. In
fact, you are almost certain to get a gold “pros-
pect ” in the crop of either bird.
On the great, lonely, southern track, which
winds through the western forest, parallel to the
ocean beach, two men were camped. They were
diggers, and returning home one evening with
about half-an-ounce of gold in the tin dish, they
placed the dish outside, and prepared for supper.
The fire was lit, and the johnny-cakes nearly
cooked, when Bill went out to get the gold. It
was gone. He asked his mate what he had done
with it. The latter denied having seen it. In-
vestigation resulted in nothing.
Suspicion breeds dislike, dislike soon becomes
hatred, and hatred is the forerunner of open
quarrel.
George was getting his sister out from home,
and he swore that Bill should never have her.
Words were freely bandied about. With mut-
tered growling each murmured recklessly, “Good
night, thief!” and turned in.
Towards three o’clock a.m. Bill dreamt that
Satan was crawling along the ridge-pole of the
tent. He awoke with a start. The flickering
gleams from the smothered fire showed movements
in the potato bag. Cautiously creeping out, he
grasped the head of the sack, and, with a shout of
triumph, roused George, with the assurance that
he had caught the thief. It was a Maori-hen.
The bird was despatched. The gizzard was
washed in the tin-dish. The result was half-an-
ounce of gold. The men grasped each other’s
hands and were friends again. The story ends as
all decent stories end, by Bill marrying George’s
sister shortly after her arrival.
POLYPE.
The Knot of Blue and Gray.
Upon my bosom lies
A knot of blue and gray ;
You ask me w'hy. Tears fill my eyes,
As low to you I say :
“ I had two brothers once,
Warm-hearted, bold, and gay ;
■They left my side. One wore the blue.
The other wore the gray.
“ One rode with Stonewall and his men
And joined his fate to Lee ;
The other followed Sherman’s march
Triumphant to the sea.
“ Both fought for what they deemed the right
And died with sword in hand ;
One sleeps amid Virginia’s hills,
And one in Georgia’s sand.
“ The same sun shines upon their graves ;
My love unchanged must stay ;
And so upon my bosom lies
The knot of blue and gray.”-
Anon.
Beware of Frauds. Drink Wolfe’s Schnapps.
The case of the Broken Hill defence
committee brings to mind that of the six Dor-
chester labourers, sentenced in 1834 to seven years
transportation, ostensibly for “ administering un-
lawful oaths,” but really for “ the crime of com-
bination.” Excitement was tremendous and
a demonstration in Copenhagen Fields (London)
was attended by 400,000 persons, and a procession
therefrom, between six and seven miles long, bear-
ing a petition of over 266,000 signatures, pro-
ceeded to the official residence of Lord Melbourne,
then Premier, to urge on him their release or the
remission of their sentences. After long delay on
the part of the Whig Ministry (backed up by the
manufacturers) the men were pardoned, but as,
according to Howell, they had on arrival at
Sydney been sold into slavery at <£l a head
(“assigned,” probably) years elapsed before they
heard of their pardon.
Writes a well-known Queensland politician
to The Bulletin
Now that Griffith has taken the Chief Justiceship, it is
likely that politics here for the next few months will in-
crease in interest to outsiders. We are certainly approach-
ins a most peculiar crisis. M'llwraith has left the province,
and wilt probably remain away until the crisis is over.
Nelson, the leader of the bogus Opposition, is spoken of as
next Speaker if the present dominant faction come out on
top at the general election. Morehead and Dickson are
both bidding hard for the vacant portfolio in the present
Government. Morehead has been appointed to go with
Griffith (who will not assume office for several months)
to the Federal Council and is on that account re-
garded as first favourite. The chances of the real Opposi-
tion winning at the election and putting a stop to black
labour and the ghastly land grant swindles, depend, I
think, on Lilley. The country could be roused, but it
requires a man with a name, leisure, and means to lead.
The Liberal party has been so starved of good men for
years past that when Griffith went over to the enemy no
one wag left who could step into his place. The labour-
party, though full of fiery enthusiasm, want constantly to
be reminded that alone they cannot do anything, and that
a policy of isolation is fatal. If Lilley (who, as you will
have seen, says his health does not permit of his entering
politics at present) came out a party would form round
him and might easily carry the election ; but whether he
would be able to keep a party together is another matter.
Marupa, the People’s
Friend.
Without question the medicines most sought
after now-a-days are those of Mother Mary Joseph
Aubert, and deservedly so, from what is heard of
their high merits everywhere. In nearly every
ease where a trial has been given relief has almost
immediately ensued. The testimonies of people
in various social positions have been forwarded to
the proprietor, and each asserts that the remedy
has truly marvellous qualities.
While that brilliant young actress, Miss Myr
Kemble, was in Wellington her voice became sa
hoarse that she was in some fear she would bo
imperfectly heard from the stage that eveninge
but Marupa was recommended, and the effect was
wonderful, she states in her testimony. ,
Mr. Walter Bentley had a similar attack, and
was afforded relief by a dose of Marupa.
Mother Mary Joseph Aubert has devoted nearly
all her life to the study of medicine. In Paris she
studied in the leading hospitals for five years, and
when she came out to New Zealand her love of
the science prompted her to commence research
among the herbs and flowers of the primeval
forests which Nature has so richly endowed our
colony with, and after 12 years of patient investi-
gation, her efforts were crowned with success and
her remedies gratefully acknowledged to be the
best and purest medicines ever offered.
They are now being introduced in Australia,
and are having the same success there as they
have met here. Mr. Kempthorne has opened a
branch in Sydney and is putting up the medicines
as fast as be can.
Meanee, Sept. 14, 1891.
Dear Sister, —I am too thankful to have the
opportunity of testifying to the genuineness of
your medicines, having given up all hopes of ever
getting better, until a friend brought you to me,
when four small bottles of your medicine cured
me, and what is more, I have been a stronger man
ever since. That was in July, 1882.
Henry Painter, senr.
Meanee, Sept. 30, 1891.
Messrs Kempthorne, Prosser and Co.
Gentlemen,— -Having used Sister Joseph’s medi-
cines for over 12 years, I can honestly say that
they cannot be surpassed.
I have seen a number of hopeless cases brought
to her and cured. She is the best, kindest, and
noblest of ladies, and never made any charge to
rich or poor, nor did it matter to what denomina-
tion you belonged you had the same kindness
shown you.
My own daughter, eleven years of age, met with
an accident, and although some of the best doctors
in Hawkes Bay despaired of saving her life, Sister
Joseph never gave up, but worked hard day and
night, and, thank God, saved her life.—Yours, &c.,
M. Young.
Napier Telegraph,
(March 25, 1892.)
Mr. H. Owen, one of our local chemists, certifies
in our columns to the benefits derived from the
use of Mother Mary Joseph Aubert’s remedies.
It is evident there is a great future before these
purely local vegetable medicines, and which we
hear are now about to be introduced into New
South Wales, Victoria, and the other Australian
Colonies, and will bring the name of New Zealand
very prominently before the public of the Southern
Continent, and be one of the best advertisements
for this Colony. The proprietor deserves success
from the spirited way in which he has entered into
this enterprise.
Christchurch Press.
(March 8, 1892.)
A Flattering Testimony. —In our advertising
columns will be found copy of a letter addressed
by Lord Onslow to Dr. de la Bachoue, the French
Consul in Wellington, in the course of which his
Lordship bears testimony to the efficacy of the
medicines manufactured by the Rev. Mother
Mary Joseph Aubert. These remedies are ex-
tracted from New Zealand plants, and Lord
Onslow states that he has found them perfectly
satisfactory and really tonic and fortifying.


The Fitzgerald Bros. (Tom and Dan) and their celebrated pony, “ Commodore,' the bed-rock of
their fortunes and a friend in need.
TOM FITZGERALD.
DAN FITZGERALD.
Thls narrative (if the Fitzgerald Brothers
and their circus experiences was fully corro-
borated by the pony, who has kindly con-
sented to stand for his portrait in The Bul-
letin. Commodore 'often nodded approv-
ingly as the two young Australians told their
yarn to the writer, and only once did Com-
modore betray an inclination to kick. That
was when the noble animal thought Brother
Tom was exaggerating financial facts. Dan
and Tom Fitzgerald, it appears, started life
as the proprietors of a little show styled
“ The Three in One ” circus, with which for
a while they travelled Austra-
lia and Maoriland, waxing in
wisdom and in “ strength.
Bad weather eventually over-
took the “Three in One” enter-
prise, and in the sultry dis-
trict of Charleville (Q.), 800
miles from Brisbane, the boys
sold the plant to pay their
liabilities and felt that the ways
of Providence are inscrutable.
Tom retired to Brisbane, and
had just relieved his feelings
with an attack of sickness, when
brother Dan wrote him to come
out into the wilderness with
his violin, whereupon Thomas
packed up the fiddle and re-
joined his relative. Being
now a long distance from no-
where, and lacking the cash
necessary to transport them-
selves, six horses and the “mas-
cotte ” Commodore to any
centre of population by rail,
they plodded overland to
Bourke, and there raised suffi-
cient wind to waft them to
Sydney.
They knocked together a new
show’, consisting of Dan, Tom,
young Duckworth, Commodore,
the six horses, a performing
goat and a waggon, and set
out to ravage the land of
their birth. Dan used to pre-
pare the meals of kangaroo,
native bear and other delicacies, while
brother Tom, who was (and is) addicted
to low-cut skirts and cravings after the
Infinite, acquired the art of “ washing-up,”
Commodore, without being asked, made a
point of wiping the utensils with his trusty
tail, and would have cooked a wallaby if
they had let him.
The first performance of this Greatest
Combination on Earth took place on a
station. The Brothers, after getting permis-
sion to camp, formed themselves into a
deputation to wait upon the rouseabouts and
arrange the price of a show Seven “ bob ’
a head being fixed as a reasonable charge for
admission, the inauguration of Fitzgerald
Bros.' new circus took place. They rigged a
piece of side-wall with, canvas, formed a
ring, and started to paralyse the public. The
entertainment opened with a four-horse act
by Duckworth (his vhaiden effort ill that
line), assisted by the strains of Tom's fiddle.
Tom, being limited to one tune, was found
to have a prejudicial effect upon the act, so
Professor Dan desired the orchestra to cease,
and the result was a veritable triumph.
Commodore was the next item on the bill.
He’s an amiable beast, and for some time he
tolerated the fiddle for the sake of the honest
hand that scraped it, but at last he
walked to the scene of the disturbance
on his hind - legs and removed the
everlasting melody with his teeth. Sub-
COMMODORE.
sequently Commodore sat upon the fiddle
and the band played never again. The
wdiole performance of this gifted pony carried
the audience by storm, and when the circus
departed with proceeds amounting to £7 the
cheers of an admiring populace rent the air.
The Fitzgeralds worked along through the
wilderness, without a band, ever finding
fresh cause-to love Commodore and magnify
his name. They have a proud recollection
of a certain “pitch” on Ounnamulla race-
course, where Tom (after a struggle with his
feelings) and took the shillings at the
entrance to the tent, and Professor Dan
climbed in tactile waggon and thumped a
kerosene tin. On this memorable night they
took £l5, and began seriously to think of
getting drunk. Anyway, they were getting
on.
Crossing couiitry to a place called lT!o
they there picked up the little darkie boy,
Harry Cardells, whose equestrian acts have
become features in the present show. By
the time they got near Bourke again they
were sufficiently flourishing for brother T<>m
to rush along as “ advance agent ! and bill
the town. The largest circus of modern
times was about to arrive. The waggon
crept into Bourke under cover of darkness,
the tent was fixed up, and the show, at 3s.
and 25., astounded the proprietors by going
straight to the local heart. Commodore’s
performance alone was declared to be cheap
at the price. Blessing Bourke and the
friends they found there the Fitzgeralds
pegged along to various places, and ter-
minated their back-block trip at Gunnedah,
with £5OO to the good. From that peaceful
township Tom Avent to Sydney and sang at
Sunday night concerts, the “ screw” helping
to keep Professor Dan and the ’osses while
he was breaking-in and training the raw
material, in readiness for a fresh start.
They made a fresh start, and have never
looked back since.
Enlarging their show from time to time as
they went along they travelled all over the
place, and put up some truly remarkable
records. For instance, their first visit to
Broken Hill brought them a solid profit of
£l5OO, and at their second visit they pulled
£350 into the tent on a Boxing-Night. At
Albafly (W.A.) they ventured to ask six
and five shillings for front and back seats,
and the steepness of this tariff caused the
tent to be generally avoided on the opening
night. Dan Fitzgerald went through with
the performance before a handful of spec-
tators. and next night Albany come forth in
one vast mob and fought for tickets an hour
before the show was advertised to begin.
Sometimes, of course, the circus has been
pitched in regions which hadn’t a population,
places where the last nan had long since
succumbed to exhaustion, and on these
occasions even Commodore couldn’t achieve
a triumph. But the Fitzgeralds
tell extraordinary tales of how
they have often been able to
fetch big audiences from long
distances away, and have taken
as much as £250 i i places that
looked most unpromising.
Moreover, one believes the
history of the Fitzgeralds as
told by themselves. There is
a George Washington appear-
ance about these two indomit-
able young Natives to btg'n
with, and the seven months'
success of the show in Mel-
bourne testifies to its money-
making ability on tour. The
Australian Circus opened quite
unpretentiously in the city of the
busted boom, and madeanaraefor
its horsey excellence right away.
Dan Fitzgerald is a born
horse-trainer, a self-taught
genius at the business, and in
a little while his four - footed
pupils are as far advanced as
they can go. Sara Bernhardt’s
pair of not very promising-
looking carriage - horses were
bought by the Fitz’s more for
advt. purposes than for tricks
in the arena, but they have
been converted into star per-
formers. If Sara could only see
one of her discarded nags jump
—well, she would buy it back
again at a fabulous price and
make it jump for her amuse-
ment until the poor thing
dropped dead, Dan Fitzgerald
behaves like a judiciously affec-
tionate parent to all his horses,
but Commodore is the apple
of his. eye. In fact, the
Brethren, jointly and severally,
love Commodore with all their
heart, and the heart of the firm is big. That
pony founded their capital, and when, in
the fulness of time, it is gathered to its
ancestors, they will put the tent into mourn-
ing and feel very bad.
# * #
Meanwhile Commodore takes his corn
reg’ler, and talks (in his own way) of com-
ing along to Sydney with the Fitzgerald
Bros., their wonderful Bronchos, and glitter*
ing company of artists, Ac. (including the
inevitable “ funny clowns,” Avho happen to
be rather funny in this instance) at Christ-
mas. Commodore, who always takes The
Bulletin when he can get hold of it, will bo
glad to see such good portraits of himself
and his young old friends.

THE DIPSOMANIAC.
You needn’t ask Balmairiiac Clark, M.P.,
the teetotal £?od, to “shout.” He always does.
In a voice like a grater. So he probably takes
nutmeg with his’n.
The cabled employment c)f Cossacks to
repress revolts by Poles is a very old plan. The
Romans used the same device and kept Africa in
ordet with legions raised in what is now Austria*
while they held down Syria with legions enrolled
in Gaul and kept the Gauls id order with armies
levied in Greece or Italy. Napoleon long worked
the oracle the same way, and fell when he departed
from the plan of holding Germany with French
troops augmented by Italian and Spanish regi-
ments, while he had his Polish lancers in Spain
and Italy. When the Skye crofters defied the
police, the British Government hesitated to send
against them the troops which were nearest,
because these were Highland regiments, so they
sent marines. Ireland has always been kept in
hand by English and Scotch regiments, while
Irish brigades are used on foreign stations to
shoot niggers, or sometimes quartered in English
manufacturing towns. If ever the British should
attempt revolution, the Indian native troops will
be brought in to furnish a contingent to the
Royalist forces, and if Australia should attempt
to assert her independence, Sikhs, Ghoorkas, and
Sepoys would be employed to “ Fire low and lay
’em out.”
Four Labour - members have been ap-
pointed to the Legislative Council of Maoriland.
When the message from the Governor reached one of
them, Mr ; Jenkinson, boilermaker, Christchurch,
he was at work inside a boiler he was at the time
constructing.—
In ancient times one day the Romans found
Great Cinetnnatus,
Their “ Only General,” behind his plough,
Drilling—potatoes
(Then a new crop), when wanted to command
The mighty forces of the Roman land !
And now, from Maoriland we have the story
Of one, a brawny toiler,
Found, when his country called him to the Senate,
Inside an iron boiler,
Curled in position where he best could settle
Each rivet firmly in the sounding metal.
And as the Ploughman of the Roman State
Was ablest far
To lead her armies on to victories great,
In times of war,
So may the boiler-maker, Jenkinson,
As a statesman, prove to be our greatest gun 1
And help to rivet to our legal code
Laws to secure
That Justice shall be as an open road
To rich and poor ;
And hammer hard at every Social wrong
To bring in time the Perfect State along.
And as he made good boilers, may he now
Make laws as good,
That will hold water, and stand wear and tear
As those have stood.
And be himself what did himself environ,
When called to legislate—good, honest iron !
Napier, M.L.
A. STEWART.

Fainting Range.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A long, low ridge that rises on the flooded Southern
side—
A smoothly-running ridge, it seems to reach the
Great Divide ;
The stunted timber tells you that the track is
clear and sound,
You can hear your footsteps ring upon the hard,
metallic ground.
The deep, dark gullies down below are lost amid
the trees
That tower up above the scrub and sway with
every breeze,
And he who knows the terrors of this trackless
sea of green
Will stop to rest and, lingering, gaze upon the
hopeless scene.
And as he journeys slowly on and climbs the
friendly rise
The dense, dark, waving field below will float
before his eyes,
And the gloomy picture holds him; wrapped in
reverie profound
Some mystic flowing current seems to lift him o’er
the ground.
It is then the voice should hasten that would speak
the warning word,
It is then the silence trembles lest the sleeper be
not stirred,
And the throbbing wakes the dreamer, and he
starts to note the change,
And stopping, stares bewildered down the top of
Fainting Range.
There are men, they say, who faltered, but, too
proud to wander back.
Unflinching faced the falling ridge and struck the
rising track—
Rising, flattening, falling, sinking, till they knew
not where to stir,
And then they dashed in madness down a never-
rising spur.
There are men amongst the travellers from the
open, boundless plain,
Where the stars at night will lead you o’er a land
that thirsts for rain,
They say the giant ranges ever mocked them in
their dreams,
And the hot wind onward sweeping urged them to
the running streams.
Tlie.e are men too weak to travel, who in restless,
fevered haste
Cared only that they left behind the dreary, level
waste —
They had no “eye for country,” and they never
knew the rise
In endless, broken, waving lino stretched to the
distant skies.
It is these and many others who have gone their
weary way,
And tempted by the troach’rous ridge their toll-
some task essay,
Stout-hearted even through the night their lonely
way have pushed
Till hope has died within them and the bravest
souls are “ bushed.”
They are ever moving past us—those who only
know defeat,
You will see them in the city, pass them in the
crowded street ;
You will see in fitful moments, marked in memory’s
mournful trace,
The long, heart-breaking stages painted on the
pallid face.
And should you meet a traveller who would reach
the Great Divide,
In pity strive to stay him till he scan the steep
straight side ;
It may be that you know no track, and all still
lingers strange,
But lift your voice in warning, ye who’ve tried by
Fainting Range 1
W. E. Oarew.
Heidelberg, Victoria.
The King of Kaloona.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Micky Morphy is King, Pope, and Providence
at Kaloona, on the head of the Diamantina, one
of the latest formed townships in the Queensland
fat west. Kaloona consists of two pubs, (rum, a
shilling a nobbier) and two stores. Micky has at
least a say in all four places.
Micky is not a J.P., but he is Chief Justice of
Kaloona. He has a Napoleonic code of his own,
and from his judgment there is no appeal. He
can enforce his sentences, for he is the adminis-
trative head of both the Judicial and Penal
departments.
“Charlie the Barman” is Judge’s Associate,
and also the Sheriff’s factotum. He carries out
the law as pronounced by Judge Micky. Micky
has but one form of punishment for offenders—
he stops their grog.
The sentences vary according to the heinousness
of the offence, from 24 hours’ stoppage to as much
as seven days! And it is all “ without the
option.”
Listen to His Honor, dealing with an offender ;
“Noa! The devil a dhrap will ye get till to-
morry noight ! It was robbing yez, ye said I was.
The devil a dhrap till thin, and not thin, unless
ye behave. Block him, Charley !’’
The dread fiat has gone forth. Kaloona holds
no snakejuice “till to-morry noight ” for the man
who dared to affront Pope Micky !
“ Av they would only adopt moy law,” says
Micky, “all the jails in Queensland would be
impty in three months.”
Micky has his sorrows. He can’t “ rade roitin’,
an’ the printin’ do be puzzlin’ ” him. He is
rather “off” in general education, and is some-
what deficient in experience of civilised luxuries.
A big squatter called in at Kaloona recently,
and Micky, as Chief Commoner, invited Trotters
to dinner, determining to astonish him with some
of the wares out of his stores. So Micky went
over for a sample of preserved Californian fruit.
Returning, he placed a tin on the table, and re-
marked— “Oidoant know whidder ye be partial
to this brand uv fruit, sorr ?”
“ Fruit, Micky? Why, that’s oysters !”
“Isthers! Isthers ! TV divil a wan o’ me
knows if that’s as good as the Pache Brand or
not. K ut we’ll try the fruit anyway.”
JOG.
The latest snake-yarn protrudes from
Hamilton (Vic.), where a thrifty and providential
reptile was found with a larder of water-rats,
opossums, and 30 eels.
The Australian Star alludes to “ the
famous King John Sobieski, of Poland, the deli-
verer of Vicuna.” Vicuna is a sort of mud and
bark hamlet in the Andes, and we should fancy
Sobieski must have been pretty tired when he got
there.
Poets should never marry. The male
canary, when shut up with his mate, finds it im-
possible to sing, and so with the poet. There
have been exceptions, but they always get away
somehow from the “hen.” Burns either did'his
composing at the plough-tail, or left his “bonny
Jean” for the stock-yard and Souter Johnny.
Tennyson had many rooms to stow away in, and
was an indefatigable walker besides, as Parkcs
knows. Not one woman in a million can allow a
man to become absorbed in composition.
The Moderator of the Melb. Presbyterian
Assembly enquires: “What is the mysterious
power that uplifts a blade of grass in defiance of
the laws of gravitation.” We suppose it is the
same power that makes a man’s hair grow out of
his head instead of into his skull. How strange
that we never see anything mysterious about the
necessity of visiting the barber —until we get bald
and try hair-restorer in vain ! And just here it
occurs to us that grass grows all the thicker and
quicker for being pressed down and bruised when
the blades begin to stick out, so the Moderator
will please notice that Nature is not too proud to
accept a little human assistance. Why a fat man,
with large feet, should be a useful article on a
young lawn is one of the most mysterious things
going.
“The Dignity of Labour!” Some one
has just published, in England, a volume dealing
with the special diseases incidental to labour.
Among them are “chimney sweep’s cancer a
sort of leprous complaint connected in some
obscure fashion with soot, “baker’s thumb,”
“washerwoman’s hand,” “stonemason’s lung,”
“potter’s rot,” “hammerman’s palsy,” “flock
fever a complaint which carries off bedding-
manufacturers, “printer’s phthisis,” “brass-
founder’s ague,” “grinder’s rot,” “ poncy ” —a
weird complaint which cuts off linen - workers,
“shoddy fever,” and “miner’s consumption.”
Also, there are strange complaints incidental to
vitriol-workers—whose teeth, hair, and nails drop
out, oil refiners, glass-blowers, sugar refiners
who are afflicted, like the deceased Herod, with
curious insects inwardly, weavers, blacksmiths,
dyers, cutlery-makers, cotton-operatives, coal-
miners, iron-workers, brewers, and a multitude of
others. Almost every trade, in fact, has some
particular horror of its own, and some of them
have six horrors or more. The dignity of labour
isn’t what it is cracked up to be.
BLIND.
(F OR THE BULLETIN.)
“ Look up to God, watch for His Light,”
Priests tell us, “in your woes and fears,”
But ah ! how many eyes there be,
Cannot see God for blinding tears ?—D. W.

The Wage-Writer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Yoil're a man df education and the morning of
your days
Has been cast in pleasant places or in literary
ikkys :
Well, you want to earn a living—’tis a thing you
have to do—
So you join the Daily Howler at a very modest
“screw.”
You will rise in your profession, so you reckon,
by-and-bye—
There’s an equal chance of rising vip to Heaven
when you die;
You will scribble weary leaders, and the subjects
of them !—Yes !
You must write on any subject when you’re
writing for the press.
You will write the Christmas stories and the
poems and the pars.,
You will hunt for new sensations and you’ll inter-
view the “stars”;,
You must know a bit of boxing, you hiust under-
stand the law,
For the “Cohen versus Moses” and the “one
Upon the jaw.” .
You must ground yourself in dogma, and on horses
aiid on “ pugs,”
On phyffdxei’d and cancer, and on aphis, bees and
bugs ;
You ’re supposed td be “acquainted ” and expected
to be ’cute
And to answer correspondence on theosophy or
fruit.
You’ll report the parson’s sernioilS rind the politi-
cian’s “views,”
You will be on hand at banquets and society
“shivoos,”
And your grammar must lie perfect and your
morals Op to date,
And your coming should be early, but your
must be late.
They will shriek at yoh for “ copy}” and despatch
you down the town,
Where you strike upon a subject, and they sweat*
and cut it down.
You may write a clever leader; aiid they sneer and
talk of “ school
You may write the way they Want you, and they
damn you for a fool.
You may hold your own opinions, and you hold
them dearly, too,
But the journal that you live on has a “ policy,
and you ?
Why, you batter those opinions for the things ymt
wear and eat,
And you sell your very virtue, like the Woman of
the street.
Then you join the “ fonVatd’’ movement, but
your faith begins to Hag-
Earning ten-and-six a column oil a democratic
rag.
You contribute to a paper, and the article you
write
Is completed in a garret, by a candle, late at
night*
You are writing for existence and you Write the
best you may,
So you “call around on Monday ” and the editor
. will say,
“We are sorry to reject it,” or, perhaps, if he’s a
brute, *
He will tell you very roughly “that the matter
doesn’t suit.”
And he hands you back your copy, and you grin
a meek “Good-bye,”
While your throat is pretty chokey and you feel
inclined to cry,
Then you take a nip of something, for it helps yoit
not to think,
And your life is not so happy that you make it
worse by drink.
Yes, I know you thought to sell it, and you did
your very best,
And you wrote it for one market, and ’tis useless
for the rest.
You had counted on acceptance, and your time is
gone to waste.
But you know you are competing with the scissors
and the paste.
Your wife at home is waiting, and she sadly needs
a dress,
But you’re going through the mill, mate, you are
xvriting for the press!
Well, the years have left you fighting, and your
youth is slipping by,
But you find, instead of resting, you must work
until you die*
Then, perhaps, your heart will fail you, or perhaps
before it breaks.
You may write a little story, half-despairing, and
it “ takes,”
Then you go up like a rocket and they pay you
what you ask,
Then you’ve “made your reputation” and ’tis
sunshine, and you bask !
Then the struggle is behind you, you forget it and
forgive,
For ’tis fame as well as money, and, by Heavens !
how you live ! !
Sydney, October, 1892.
E. J. BRADY.
